CASE_ID: 122
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Cellulitis

ALIASES:
skin infection
soft tissue infection
bacterial cellulitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man with diabetes and peripheral vascular disease presents with 3 days of progressive redness, warmth, and swelling of his left lower leg following a minor abrasion while gardening.
2. The redness has expanded rapidly and he now has a red line tracking up his leg; he is febrile at 38.9°C.
3. On exam, the left lower leg shows a well-demarcated area of erythema, edema, and warmth extending from the ankle to mid-calf; a red streak (lymphangitis) tracks proximally toward the groin.
4. WBC is 18,200; blood cultures are drawn; no fluctuance or crepitus is appreciated; there is no evidence of abscess formation on bedside ultrasound.
5. He is started on IV cefazolin given systemic signs and lymphangitis; blood cultures grow beta-hemolytic Streptococcus pyogenes (Group A Strep).
6. After 48 hours of IV antibiotics with clinical improvement (decreased erythema and fever), he is transitioned to oral cephalexin for a total 14-day course with wound care and leg elevation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common causes: Group A Streptococcus (S. pyogenes) and Staphylococcus aureus.
- Non-purulent cellulitis: streptococcal; purulent (abscess, furuncle): staphylococcal (cover MRSA if needed).
- Lymphangitis (red streak) indicates spread along lymphatics; systemic signs require IV antibiotics.
- Risk factors: skin disruption, edema, diabetes, obesity, tinea pedis, venous insufficiency.
- Mark borders of erythema with pen to monitor progression; failure to improve in 48 hours = consider deeper infection (necrotizing fasciitis).

CASE_ID: 123
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Osteomyelitis

ALIASES:
bone infection
hematogenous osteomyelitis
chronic osteomyelitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 12-year-old boy presents with 5 days of fever, severe right leg pain, and refusal to bear weight; he had a minor skin infection on his leg 3 weeks ago.
2. On exam, he has point tenderness over the distal right femoral metaphysis with soft tissue swelling and warmth; temperature is 39.2°C.
3. WBC is 18,800; ESR is 86 mm/hr; CRP is markedly elevated; plain X-rays of the femur are normal at this stage.
4. Blood cultures are drawn; MRI of the right femur shows bone marrow edema, periosteal elevation, and a small subperiosteal abscess at the distal femoral metaphysis.
5. Blood cultures grow Staphylococcus aureus (MSSA); IV nafcillin is started.
6. After 3 days of IV antibiotics with clinical improvement, he is transitioned to oral clindamycin to complete a 4-week course; repeat MRI at 6 weeks shows resolution of the subperiosteal abscess without surgical drainage required.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause: Staphylococcus aureus (all ages); Salmonella in sickle cell disease; Group B Strep in neonates.
- Hematogenous spread most common in children; metaphysis is most commonly affected (end-arteriole loops).
- Plain X-rays lag 10-14 days; MRI is the most sensitive and specific early imaging.
- Brodie abscess: subacute osteomyelitis presenting as a well-circumscribed radiolucent lesion.
- Treatment: IV antibiotics 4-6 weeks; surgical drainage if abscess present or no improvement.

CASE_ID: 124
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Gonorrhea

ALIASES:
Neisseria gonorrhoeae
gonococcal infection
gonorrhoea
disseminated gonococcal infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old sexually active man presents with 5 days of purulent urethral discharge, dysuria, and urethral itching.
2. He reports unprotected intercourse with multiple partners over the past month; he has not been tested for STIs in 2 years.
3. On exam, copious yellow-green purulent discharge is expressed from the urethral meatus; there is no scrotal swelling or epididymal tenderness.
4. Gram stain of the urethral discharge shows intracellular gram-negative diplococci within neutrophils.
5. Urine NAAT (nucleic acid amplification test) is positive for Neisseria gonorrhoeae; testing for concurrent Chlamydia trachomatis is also performed and returns positive.
6. He is treated with ceftriaxone 500 mg IM plus doxycycline 100 mg twice daily for 7 days (treating both gonorrhea and chlamydia); all sexual partners from the past 60 days are notified.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common bacterial STI in the US (with chlamydia); Gram-negative diplococci.
- Males: urethritis (discharge, dysuria); Females: often asymptomatic or cervicitis, PID.
- Gram stain showing intracellular diplococci is highly specific for gonorrhea in men.
- Disseminated gonococcal infection (DGI): triad of migratory polyarthralgia, tenosynovitis, and skin pustules on an erythematous base.
- Treatment: ceftriaxone (always treat chlamydia co-infection empirically with doxycycline).

CASE_ID: 125
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Fibromyalgia

ALIASES:
fibromyalgia syndrome
FMS
chronic widespread pain

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old woman presents with a 3-year history of widespread musculoskeletal pain involving her entire body, severe fatigue, and unrefreshing sleep despite sleeping 9-10 hours per night.
2. She reports cognitive difficulties ("fibro fog"), morning stiffness lasting 30 minutes, headaches, irritable bowel symptoms, and bladder urgency.
3. On exam, she has diffuse tenderness to light palpation at multiple sites including the trapezius, suboccipital, lateral epicondyles, and knees bilaterally; there is no joint swelling, warmth, or synovitis.
4. CBC, CMP, ESR, CRP, ANA, RF, TSH, and creatine kinase are all normal or negative.
5. The widespread pain index (WPI) is 12 and symptom severity score (SSS) is 9, meeting ACR 2010 diagnostic criteria for fibromyalgia.
6. She is started on a multimodal treatment program including graded aerobic exercise, cognitive behavioral therapy, and duloxetine (SNRI); at 3 months she reports moderate improvement in pain and functioning.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Central sensitization disorder with diffuse musculoskeletal pain, fatigue, sleep disturbance, cognitive symptoms.
- Diagnosis: widespread pain (≥4 of 5 body regions) + symptom duration ≥3 months + no other explanation; NO specific laboratory test or imaging finding.
- Normal inflammatory markers and normal joint exam differentiate from inflammatory arthritis.
- Associated with: irritable bowel, interstitial cystitis, migraine, depression, anxiety.
- Treatment: aerobic exercise (best evidence), CBT, low-dose tricyclics (amitriptyline), SNRIs (duloxetine), gabapentin/pregabalin.

CASE_ID: 126
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Osteoporosis

ALIASES:
bone loss
decreased bone density
osteopenia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old woman presents after a low-impact fall from standing height resulted in severe right hip pain; she was unable to bear weight after the fall.
2. She is postmenopausal, has smoked half a pack of cigarettes daily for 30 years, and has taken prednisone for rheumatoid arthritis for 10 years.
3. On exam, the right leg is shortened and externally rotated; she is in significant pain with any movement.
4. X-ray of the right hip shows a displaced right femoral neck fracture; she is taken to the operating room for right total hip arthroplasty.
5. DEXA scan performed postoperatively shows a T-score of -3.2 at the lumbar spine and -2.8 at the hip, consistent with osteoporosis.
6. She is started on alendronate plus calcium and vitamin D supplementation; fall prevention strategies and physical therapy are arranged.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- T-score: normal ≥-1.0; osteopenia -1.0 to -2.5; osteoporosis ≤-2.5 on DEXA scan.
- Risk factors: postmenopausal, female, advanced age, corticosteroids, smoking, low BMI, family history, calcium/vitamin D deficiency.
- Most common fragility fracture sites: vertebrae (most common), hip (most morbidity/mortality), wrist (Colles fracture).
- Treatment: bisphosphonates (alendronate, first-line); calcium + vitamin D; denosumab, teriparatide for severe disease.
- Glucocorticoid-induced osteoporosis: bisphosphonate if on steroids >3 months.

CASE_ID: 127
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Anorexia Nervosa

ALIASES:
anorexia
restrictive eating disorder
AN

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 17-year-old girl is brought by her parents who report she has lost 25 pounds over 6 months, eats only tiny amounts, and exercises 3 hours daily despite fatigue.
2. She denies any problem with her weight and insists she is "too fat" despite appearing severely underweight; her BMI is 15.2.
3. On exam, she has lanugo hair on her face and arms, dry skin, yellowish skin discoloration, bradycardia at 46 bpm, and hypotension at 88/58 mmHg.
4. Labs show hypokalemia 2.8 mEq/L, hypophosphatemia, low estrogen, elevated BUN (prerenal from poor intake), and mildly elevated transaminases.
5. ECG shows prolonged QTc interval and sinus bradycardia; bone density scan shows osteoporosis with a Z-score of -2.4 for her age.
6. She is admitted for medically supervised nutritional rehabilitation; a multidisciplinary team including psychiatry, nutrition, and family therapy is initiated; refeeding syndrome is monitored for carefully during nutritional replenishment.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Restriction of energy intake + intense fear of weight gain + distorted body image; lowest mortality of mental illness but highest mortality of eating disorders.
- Medical complications: bradycardia, hypotension, lanugo, amenorrhea, osteoporosis, hypokalemia, hypophosphatemia.
- Refeeding syndrome: electrolyte shifts (hypophosphatemia most dangerous) when refeeding malnourished patients — monitor closely.
- Distinguish from bulimia: anorexia = restriction + low weight; bulimia = binge-purge + often normal weight.
- Treatment: nutritional rehabilitation (priority), CBT, family-based therapy; no FDA-approved medication for anorexia.

CASE_ID: 128
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Borderline Personality Disorder

ALIASES:
BPD
emotionally unstable personality disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old woman presents to the ED for the fourth time this year following a superficial self-cutting episode on her forearms after a fight with her boyfriend.
2. She describes intense fear of abandonment; her relationships are described as alternating between idealization ("he's perfect") and devaluation ("he's the worst person I've ever met") within the same week.
3. She reports a chronically empty feeling, impulsive spending and substance use, and recurrent self-harm as a way to cope with emotional pain.
4. Her affect is labile throughout the interview; she becomes angry when the interviewer appears to end the session, accusing them of abandoning her.
5. Medical causes of mood lability are ruled out; urine toxicology is negative; she does not meet criteria for bipolar disorder given no discrete manic or hypomanic episodes.
6. She is referred for dialectical behavior therapy (DBT), which specifically targets emotional dysregulation and self-harm behaviors; she is linked with an outpatient DBT program.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pervasive pattern of instability in relationships, self-image, affect, and impulse control.
- Nine criteria (need 5): fear of abandonment, unstable relationships (splitting), unstable identity, impulsivity, self-harm/suicidal behavior, affective instability, chronic emptiness, anger, transient paranoia/dissociation.
- Splitting: black-or-white thinking about others (idealization vs. devaluation).
- Self-harm is often a maladaptive coping mechanism, not always suicidal intent.
- Treatment: dialectical behavior therapy (DBT) is gold standard; no FDA-approved medication but SSRIs/mood stabilizers used adjunctively.

CASE_ID: 129
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acne Vulgaris

ALIASES:
acne
comedonal acne
nodulocystic acne

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 16-year-old boy presents requesting treatment for facial pimples that have been worsening over the past year; he reports significant social anxiety because of his skin.
2. His father had severe acne as a teenager; he washes his face twice daily but the lesions continue to worsen.
3. On exam, he has comedones (open and closed), inflammatory papules, pustules, and three large tender nodules (>5mm) on his cheeks and jaw; the forehead and nose are also affected.
4. No scarring is present yet; the lesions are limited to the face and upper chest.
5. Given the presence of nodular lesions and significant psychological impact, he is classified as having severe nodulocystic acne.
6. He is started on oral isotretinoin after counseling regarding the iPLEDGE program requirements, teratogenicity, monitoring of liver function and triglycerides, and mood monitoring; at 5 months the nodular lesions have resolved completely.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Multifactorial: androgen-stimulated sebum production, follicular plugging, Cutibacterium acnes overgrowth, inflammation.
- Four lesion types: comedones (open=blackheads, closed=whiteheads), papules, pustules, nodules/cysts.
- Treatment ladder: topical retinoids + benzoyl peroxide (mild) → add topical antibiotics (moderate) → oral antibiotics or for females oral contraceptives (moderate-severe) → isotretinoin (severe/nodular/refractory).
- Isotretinoin: teratogenic (iPLEDGE), causes dry skin/lips, elevated LFTs and triglycerides, psychiatric effects.

CASE_ID: 130
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Tinea Capitis

ALIASES:
scalp ringworm
dermatophyte scalp infection
tinea of the scalp

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 7-year-old boy is brought in for evaluation of patchy hair loss with scaling on his scalp that has been spreading over the past 3 weeks.
2. Several of his classmates have had similar problems; his younger sister has also developed scalp scaling.
3. On exam, there are two areas of broken-off hairs ("black dot" pattern), scaling, and subtle erythema on his scalp; cervical lymphadenopathy is present bilaterally.
4. Wood lamp examination shows greenish fluorescence of affected hair shafts.
5. Potassium hydroxide (KOH) preparation of plucked hairs shows endothrix pattern with fungal spores within the hair shaft; fungal culture grows Trichophyton tonsurans.
6. He is started on oral griseofulvin for 8 weeks (topical antifungals alone are insufficient for tinea capitis) with selenium sulfide shampoo to reduce spore spread; his sister is also treated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Dermatophyte infection of the scalp and hair follicles; most common in prepubertal children.
- Most common cause in the US: Trichophyton tonsurans (endothrix — spores within hair shaft).
- Clinical presentations: scaly alopecia, black dot (broken hairs), kerion (boggy, painful inflammatory plaque — superinfected).
- Diagnosis: KOH prep of hair + fungal culture; Wood lamp (green fluorescence with Microsporum species — NOT T. tonsurans).
- Treatment: systemic antifungals required (oral griseofulvin or terbinafine); topical alone is inadequate.

CASE_ID: 131
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Meconium Aspiration Syndrome

ALIASES:
MAS
meconium aspiration
meconium-stained amniotic fluid

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-week postterm male neonate is delivered via emergency cesarean section for fetal distress; the amniotic fluid is thick, pea-soup colored (meconium-stained).
2. At delivery, the infant is limp, cyanotic, and apneic; meconium is visible below the vocal cords on direct laryngoscopy.
3. After initial resuscitation with positive pressure ventilation, he develops worsening respiratory distress with grunting, nasal flaring, and intercostal retractions.
4. Chest X-ray shows coarse, irregular patchy infiltrates with areas of hyperinflation and bilateral pneumothorax on the right side.
5. Blood gas shows pH 7.18, pCO2 58, pO2 42 on FiO2 of 1.0 with worsening respiratory failure.
6. He is intubated and placed on high-frequency oscillatory ventilation; inhaled nitric oxide is added for persistent pulmonary hypertension; he requires ECMO and recovers after 10 days of intensive support.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Postterm neonates at highest risk; meconium in amniotic fluid occurs in ~10-15% of deliveries.
- Airway obstruction (ball-valve effect) → air trapping → pneumothorax; chemical pneumonitis; pulmonary hypertension.
- CXR: coarse irregular infiltrates, hyperinflation, atelectasis, pneumothorax.
- Persistent pulmonary hypertension of the newborn (PPHN): right-to-left shunting through PDA and foramen ovale → severe hypoxia.
- Treatment: intubation, surfactant, high-frequency ventilation, inhaled nitric oxide, ECMO if refractory.

CASE_ID: 132
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Congenital Hypothyroidism

ALIASES:
cretinism
neonatal hypothyroidism
congenital thyroid deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-week-old female infant presents for evaluation after her state newborn screening returned an elevated TSH; she was born at term and appeared well at discharge.
2. Her parents report she is very sleepy, difficult to wake for feedings, has a hoarse cry, and has not regained her birth weight.
3. On exam, she has a large anterior fontanelle, macroglossia, umbilical hernia, dry mottled skin, and hypotonia; she appears puffy and has a hoarse cry.
4. Repeat TSH is 142 mIU/L (markedly elevated); free T4 is undetectable; thyroid ultrasound shows an absent thyroid gland (athyreosis).
5. Bone age X-ray shows delayed skeletal maturation; technetium thyroid scan confirms complete absence of functioning thyroid tissue.
6. Levothyroxine is started immediately at 10-15 mcg/kg/day; with early treatment she achieves normal neurodevelopmental milestones at 12-month follow-up.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common preventable cause of intellectual disability worldwide; detected by newborn screening (TSH + T4).
- Most common cause: thyroid dysgenesis (aplasia, ectopia); less common: inborn errors of thyroid hormone synthesis (goitrous hypothyroidism).
- Classic features if untreated: large tongue, umbilical hernia, large fontanelle, constipation, hypotonia, poor feeding, hoarse cry, jaundice, intellectual disability (cretinism).
- Treatment must begin within 2 weeks of birth to prevent irreversible neurological damage.

CASE_ID: 133
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Ovarian Torsion

ALIASES:
adnexal torsion
torsion of the ovary

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old woman presents to the ED with sudden onset severe right lower quadrant pain that began 4 hours ago while she was exercising; she has nausea and has vomited twice.
2. She had a right ovarian cyst noted on ultrasound 2 months ago measuring 5.2 cm; she is not pregnant.
3. On exam, she is in significant distress with a heart rate of 112 bpm; right lower quadrant and right adnexal tenderness is exquisite; there is no peritoneal rebound.
4. Urine pregnancy test is negative; WBC is 12,400; CRP is mildly elevated.
5. Pelvic ultrasound shows an enlarged right ovary measuring 8.2 cm with a large cyst; Doppler imaging shows absent venous flow and markedly decreased arterial flow to the right ovary.
6. She undergoes emergency laparoscopic detorsion within 4 hours of presentation; the right ovary is found to be twisted 720 degrees but is viable after untwisting; the cyst is removed and the ovary is preserved.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Surgical emergency; ovarian/adnexal torsion most common in reproductive-age women; risk increased with ovarian masses (especially >5 cm, dermoid cysts).
- Sudden onset severe unilateral pelvic pain + nausea/vomiting + adnexal mass.
- Doppler may show absent flow but CANNOT rule out torsion (flow can be present even with torsion).
- Do NOT wait for laboratory results if clinical suspicion is high — go directly to OR.
- Detorsion (untwisting) is preferred over salpingo-oophorectomy; ovary often recovers even if appears necrotic.

CASE_ID: 134
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Placenta Previa

ALIASES:
previa
low-lying placenta
complete placenta previa

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman at 30 weeks gestation presents to the ED with sudden onset painless bright red vaginal bleeding; she soaked two pads over the past hour.
2. She has had two prior cesarean sections; she denies contractions, trauma, or rupture of membranes.
3. On exam, the uterus is soft and non-tender; fetal heart rate is 148 bpm and reactive; blood pressure is 108/68 mmHg.
4. A digital cervical exam is NOT performed given suspected placenta previa; transabdominal ultrasound shows the placenta completely covering the internal cervical os.
5. Hemoglobin is 9.8 g/dL; she receives a blood transfusion and is admitted for monitoring; the bleeding slows spontaneously.
6. She is managed expectantly with pelvic rest; at 36 weeks a repeat hemorrhage necessitates emergency cesarean delivery with delivery of a viable male infant; accreta is noted at the prior uterine scar site.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Placenta covering (complete) or near (marginal) the internal cervical os.
- Classic: painless bright red vaginal bleeding in the third trimester — placenta previa until proven otherwise.
- NEVER perform digital cervical exam (risk of catastrophic hemorrhage).
- Risk factors: prior cesarean/uterine surgery, multiparity, advanced maternal age, prior placenta previa.
- Placenta accreta: abnormal invasion into uterine wall; risk increases with prior cesarean + previa.
- Management: expectant if stable; cesarean delivery (vaginal delivery is contraindicated).

CASE_ID: 135
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Serotonin Syndrome

ALIASES:
serotonin toxicity
serotonin toxidrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman on fluoxetine for depression is started on tramadol by her orthopedic surgeon for knee pain; 4 hours after her first tramadol dose she becomes agitated and confused.
2. She is febrile at 39.2°C, diaphoretic, and her heart rate is 138 bpm.
3. On exam, she has bilateral lower extremity clonus, hyperreflexia, bilateral mydriasis, and marked agitation; her muscle tone is increased throughout.
4. The symptom onset was abrupt and closely related to the addition of tramadol to her regimen.
5. All serotonergic medications are immediately discontinued; serum CK is 1,200 U/L; toxicology screen is negative for illicit substances.
6. She is given IV benzodiazepines for agitation and cyproheptadine (serotonin 2A antagonist) orally; symptoms resolve completely over 24 hours without recurrence.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Excess serotonergic activity; classic triad: altered mental status + autonomic instability + neuromuscular abnormalities (clonus is KEY).
- Common drug combinations: SSRI + MAOI (most dangerous), SSRI + tramadol/linezolid/triptans, SSRI + dextromethorphan.
- Rapid onset (usually within 24 hours of drug addition).
- Distinguished from NMS: clonus/hyperreflexia (vs. lead-pipe rigidity); rapid onset (vs. gradual); serotonergic drugs (vs. dopamine antagonists).
- Treatment: discontinue offending agents; benzodiazepines; cyproheptadine (5-HT2 antagonist); supportive care.

CASE_ID: 136
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Digoxin Toxicity

ALIASES:
digitalis toxicity
digoxin overdose

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 82-year-old woman with heart failure and atrial fibrillation on digoxin presents with 2 days of nausea, vomiting, and seeing yellow-green halos around lights and objects.
2. She recently started clarithromycin for a respiratory infection without dose adjustment of her digoxin.
3. On exam, heart rate is 42 bpm and irregular; she is nauseated and complains of blurred, yellow-tinted vision.
4. ECG shows atrial fibrillation with slow ventricular response, bidirectional ventricular tachycardia, and scooped ST depression ("Salvador Dali mustache" pattern) in the lateral leads.
5. Serum digoxin level is 4.2 ng/mL (therapeutic range 0.5-2.0 ng/mL); potassium is 5.8 mEq/L.
6. Digoxin-specific antibody fragments (Digibind) are administered with resolution of arrhythmias and improvement in symptoms over 6 hours; clarithromycin is discontinued.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Digoxin inhibits Na/K-ATPase; increased intracellular calcium → increased inotropy; increased vagal tone → decreased AV conduction.
- Toxicity: nausea/vomiting, xanthopsia (yellow-green vision), arrhythmias (bradycardia, heart block, bidirectional VT).
- ECG: scooped ST depression, bidirectional VT (pathognomonic of severe toxicity).
- Hyperkalemia worsens toxicity; hypokalemia predisposes to toxicity.
- Drug interactions: clarithromycin, amiodarone, verapamil (increase digoxin levels by inhibiting P-glycoprotein).
- Antidote: digoxin-specific antibody fragments (Digibind/DigiFab).

CASE_ID: 137
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lithium Toxicity

ALIASES:
lithium overdose
lithium poisoning

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 44-year-old man with bipolar disorder on lithium presents with a 2-day history of coarse tremor, slurred speech, ataxia, and confusion.
2. He recently started ibuprofen for a knee injury without telling his psychiatrist; he also had a gastrointestinal illness with diarrhea and decreased oral intake last week.
3. On exam, he has a coarse resting and action tremor, dysarthria, ataxic gait, hyperreflexia, and cognitive slowing.
4. Serum lithium level is 2.8 mEq/L (therapeutic range 0.6-1.2 mEq/L); serum creatinine is 2.4 mg/dL (baseline 0.9).
5. NSAIDs reduced renal prostaglandins, causing lithium retention; dehydration further elevated serum lithium.
6. Ibuprofen is discontinued; aggressive IV normal saline hydration is administered; given his severe neurological symptoms and level of 2.8, hemodialysis is performed with complete neurological recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Lithium has a narrow therapeutic index (0.6-1.2 mEq/L); renal elimination; no hepatic metabolism.
- Toxicity symptoms: BRAIN — tremor (coarse), confusion, ataxia, nystagmus, seizures; also GI (nausea, vomiting, diarrhea).
- Drugs that increase lithium levels: NSAIDs (reduce GFR), thiazide diuretics, ACE inhibitors (all reduce renal clearance).
- Dehydration and sodium depletion increase lithium reabsorption (lithium follows sodium in proximal tubule).
- Treatment: IV fluids, stop offending agents; hemodialysis for severe toxicity (level >3.5 or severe symptoms).

CASE_ID: 138
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Central Retinal Artery Occlusion

ALIASES:
CRAO
retinal artery occlusion
eye stroke

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man with hypertension, diabetes, and atrial fibrillation presents with sudden complete painless vision loss in his right eye that developed instantly while reading; it has been 45 minutes since onset.
2. He denies eye pain, headache, or any preceding visual symptoms.
3. On exam, visual acuity in the right eye is hand-motion only; the left eye is normal; the right pupil has a relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD).
4. Fundoscopy of the right eye shows diffuse retinal whitening (ischemic edema) with a cherry-red spot at the fovea and a pale optic disc; the retinal arterioles appear attenuated.
5. Intraocular pressure is normal; emergent workup reveals an embolic source on transesophageal echocardiography showing a mobile thrombus in the left atrial appendage.
6. Despite ocular massage, anterior chamber paracentesis, and emergent fibrinolysis, visual recovery is minimal; he is anticoagulated for the underlying atrial fibrillation to prevent further embolic events.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Sudden, painless, complete monocular vision loss — ocular emergency (golden window <90-120 minutes).
- Classic fundus findings: retinal whitening (ischemic edema) + cherry-red spot at fovea (fovea supplied by choroid) + pale disc + attenuated vessels.
- RAPD (Marcus Gunn pupil) present in affected eye.
- Most common causes: atherosclerotic embolus (Hollenhorst plaque), cardioembolic (AFib), carotid disease.
- Treatment window is very narrow; outcomes poor despite intervention; workup for embolic source is essential.

CASE_ID: 139
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: ophthalmology

DIAGNOSIS:
Glaucoma

ALIASES:
open-angle glaucoma
primary open-angle glaucoma
POAG
chronic glaucoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old African American man presents for a routine eye exam; he denies any visual symptoms but his brother was recently diagnosed with glaucoma.
2. He has no eye pain or redness; he is not aware of any vision changes.
3. On exam, intraocular pressure (IOP) is 28 mmHg in both eyes (normal <21); slit-lamp exam shows a wide-open anterior chamber angle bilaterally on gonioscopy.
4. Fundoscopy shows an enlarged cup-to-disc ratio of 0.8 bilaterally (normal <0.5) with inferior neuroretinal rim thinning and notching.
5. Humphrey visual field testing reveals characteristic arcuate scotomas (nerve fiber bundle defects) and nasal step defects bilaterally — consistent with glaucomatous visual field loss.
6. He is started on topical timolol (beta-blocker) plus latanoprost (prostaglandin analog); IOP decreases to 16 mmHg and disease progression is halted at 2-year follow-up.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause of irreversible blindness worldwide; often asymptomatic until advanced.
- Primary open-angle glaucoma: elevated IOP + open angle on gonioscopy + optic nerve damage + visual field loss.
- Optic nerve: cup-to-disc ratio >0.5, asymmetry, inferior/superior notching are suspicious.
- Visual field: arcuate scotoma, nasal step, tunnel vision (late).
- Risk factors: African American race, family history, age, elevated IOP, thin cornea, myopia.
- Treatment: topical eye drops (prostaglandin analogs = first-line); beta-blockers, CAIs; laser; surgery.

CASE_ID: 140
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Mesenteric Ischemia

ALIASES:
mesenteric ischemia
intestinal ischemia
bowel ischemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 74-year-old man with atrial fibrillation and heart failure presents with sudden onset severe periumbilical pain that is out of proportion to his physical exam findings; the pain began 3 hours ago.
2. He reports nausea and vomiting; he has had two loose stools.
3. On exam, his abdomen is soft with mild diffuse tenderness; there is no guarding or rigidity despite his pain being 9/10 in severity — "pain out of proportion to exam" is noted.
4. WBC is 22,000; lactate is 4.8 mmol/L; metabolic acidosis is present on ABG.
5. CT angiography of the abdomen shows a filling defect in the superior mesenteric artery consistent with embolism, with bowel wall thickening and pneumatosis intestinalis in the small bowel.
6. He is taken emergently to interventional radiology for catheter-directed thrombolysis followed by surgical exploration, which reveals approximately 60 cm of necrotic small bowel requiring resection; he is anticoagulated postoperatively.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Surgical emergency; most common cause: embolism to superior mesenteric artery (often from AFib or cardiac thrombus).
- Classic: severe abdominal pain out of proportion to physical exam; nausea/vomiting/diarrhea (early); bloody stools (late).
- Late findings: peritonitis, fever, elevated lactate, metabolic acidosis = bowel infarction (poor prognosis).
- CT angiography is diagnostic; immediate surgical or endovascular intervention required.
- "Fear the gut that looks better than the patient feels."

CASE_ID: 141
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Bowel Obstruction

ALIASES:
small bowel obstruction
SBO
intestinal obstruction

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old woman presents with 2 days of crampy abdominal pain, nausea, bilious vomiting, and an inability to pass gas or stool.
2. She had an open appendectomy 15 years ago; she has not had bowel movements in 2 days.
3. On exam, her abdomen is distended and mildly tender diffusely; high-pitched, rushing bowel sounds are heard; there is no peritoneal rebound or guarding.
4. Plain abdominal X-ray shows multiple dilated loops of small bowel with air-fluid levels in a stepladder pattern; there is an absence of colonic gas.
5. CT scan of the abdomen and pelvis confirms a transition point in the mid-ileum with a focal adhesion band causing complete small bowel obstruction; no signs of ischemia or strangulation are present.
6. She is managed conservatively with NGT decompression, IV fluids, and bowel rest; she passes flatus at 36 hours and the obstruction resolves without surgical intervention.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause in adults: adhesions from prior surgery (most common), followed by hernias and malignancy.
- Classic: colicky abdominal pain, nausea/vomiting (bilious if distal to duodenum), obstipation, distension.
- Plain X-ray: dilated small bowel loops, air-fluid levels, absent colonic gas; CT is more sensitive/specific.
- Strangulation (compromised blood supply): fever, constant severe pain, peritonitis, leukocytosis = surgical emergency.
- Management: NGT decompression + IV fluids; surgery if strangulation, complete obstruction not resolving, or closed-loop obstruction.

CASE_ID: 142
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Inguinal Hernia

ALIASES:
indirect inguinal hernia
direct inguinal hernia
groin hernia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 55-year-old man presents with a 6-month history of a bulge in his right groin that appears when he coughs or strains and disappears when he lies down.
2. He works as a construction laborer and notices the bulge is worse at the end of the workday; it is associated with a dull aching discomfort.
3. On exam, a reducible bulge is palpable in the right inguinal region that increases with Valsalva; the bulge extends into the right scrotum.
4. The hernia exits through the deep inguinal ring, passes through the inguinal canal, and emerges through the external inguinal ring into the scrotum — consistent with an indirect hernia.
5. He is asked to lift a heavy object in the office; the bulge becomes prominent and non-reducible; over 30 minutes it reduces again with gentle pressure and patient positioning.
6. He is scheduled for elective laparoscopic inguinal hernia repair (TEP) given his symptoms; at surgery a patent processus vaginalis is confirmed as the hernia sac.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common hernia; most common hernia in both males and females.
- Indirect: exits through deep inguinal ring → inguinal canal → external ring → scrotum; congenital (patent processus vaginalis); lateral to inferior epigastric vessels.
- Direct: exits through Hesselbach triangle (defect in posterior inguinal wall); medial to inferior epigastric vessels; acquired.
- Complications: incarceration (cannot reduce), strangulation (ischemia — emergency surgery).
- Treatment: surgical repair (open or laparoscopic); watchful waiting only for asymptomatic direct hernias.

CASE_ID: 143
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Necrotizing Fasciitis

ALIASES:
flesh eating bacteria
necrotizing soft tissue infection
fournier gangrene

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old diabetic man presents with rapidly spreading pain, swelling, and redness of his left leg that began 2 days after a minor puncture wound; the pain is far worse than the skin appearance suggests.
2. He is febrile at 39.8°C, heart rate is 126 bpm, and blood pressure is 92/60 mmHg; he appears toxic.
3. On exam, the skin overlying the left leg has wooden-hard induration beneath the skin; crepitus is felt on palpation; patches of skin discoloration and blistering are present.
4. WBC is 28,000; creatinine is 2.8 mg/dL; lactate is 5.2 mmol/L; blood cultures are drawn.
5. CT scan of the leg shows gas tracking along fascial planes with soft tissue thickening and fluid collections.
6. He is taken emergently to the operating room within 1 hour; surgical exploration reveals gray, necrotic fascia with dishwater-colored fluid that does not bleed — classic findings; aggressive debridement is performed and broad-spectrum antibiotics are started; he requires two additional debridements over 5 days.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Surgical emergency; rapidly progressive infection of deep fascial planes with high mortality if treatment delayed.
- Classic: severe pain out of proportion to exam, wooden-hard induration, crepitus (gas), skin necrosis, systemic toxicity.
- Risk factors: diabetes, immunosuppression, obesity, peripheral vascular disease.
- CT: gas along fascial planes is pathognomonic; do NOT delay surgery for imaging if diagnosis is suspected.
- Treatment: immediate aggressive surgical debridement + broad-spectrum antibiotics (piperacillin-tazobactam + vancomycin); hyperbaric oxygen adjunct.

CASE_ID: 144
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Pelvic Inflammatory Disease

ALIASES:
PID
salpingitis
tubo-ovarian abscess

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 21-year-old sexually active woman presents with 3 days of bilateral lower abdominal pain, vaginal discharge, and fever.
2. She has had multiple sexual partners in the past year and inconsistently uses barrier contraception; her last STI screen was 18 months ago.
3. On exam, temperature is 38.6°C; cervical motion tenderness (chandelier sign), uterine tenderness, and bilateral adnexal tenderness are present on bimanual exam.
4. WBC is 16,200; ESR and CRP are elevated; vaginal wet prep shows increased WBCs; NAAT testing is positive for Neisseria gonorrhoeae.
5. Transvaginal ultrasound shows a 4.2 cm right adnexal complex mass consistent with a tubo-ovarian abscess; no free peritoneal fluid is seen.
6. She is admitted for IV cefoxitin plus doxycycline; given the tubo-ovarian abscess, metronidazole is added; the abscess decreases in size over 5 days and she is discharged on oral antibiotics with instructions on partner notification.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Ascending infection from lower genital tract; most common pathogens: N. gonorrhoeae, C. trachomatis, and vaginal anaerobes.
- Minimum criteria: cervical motion tenderness + uterine tenderness + adnexal tenderness in sexually active woman.
- Complications: tubo-ovarian abscess, chronic pelvic pain, ectopic pregnancy, infertility (each episode increases risk).
- Chandelier sign: cervical motion tenderness so severe patient "jumps to the chandelier."
- Treatment: ceftriaxone + doxycycline + metronidazole; IV antibiotics for TOA, severe disease, or pregnancy.

CASE_ID: 145
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Nursemaid Elbow

ALIASES:
radial head subluxation
pulled elbow
annular ligament displacement

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-year-old girl is brought to the ED by her father who reports he grabbed her hand to prevent her from running into traffic; immediately afterward she began crying and refuses to move her left arm.
2. She holds her left arm slightly flexed and pronated; she will not allow anyone to touch it and cries if the arm is moved.
3. On exam, she is in no distress when the arm is held still; there is no swelling, ecchymosis, or bony tenderness; she refuses any supination or flexion of the left elbow.
4. X-rays of the left elbow are obtained given her young age and show no fracture; the radiocapitellar line is normal.
5. The pediatric emergency physician performs a hyperpronation maneuver; a subtle click is felt over the radial head and within 10 minutes the child is spontaneously using her arm to reach for toys.
6. The father is counseled about the mechanism of injury and advised not to swing the child by her arms; no immobilization is needed.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common elbow injury in children ages 1-4; caused by sudden longitudinal traction on an outstretched arm.
- Child holds arm in slight flexion and pronation and refuses to use it; no bony tenderness, no swelling.
- Annular ligament slips over the radial head and becomes trapped in the radioulnar joint.
- Treatment: supination-flexion or hyperpronation maneuver; feel/hear a click; child uses arm within minutes.
- X-rays are normal; diagnosis is clinical.

CASE_ID: 146
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cluster Headache

ALIASES:
cluster headaches
trigeminal autonomic cephalalgia
suicide headache

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man presents with his wife who reports he has had severe episodes of unilateral right-sided headaches occurring once or twice daily for the past 3 weeks, always around 2 AM.
2. He describes each attack as an excruciating stabbing pain around his right eye lasting 45-90 minutes; he paces the room in agony during attacks.
3. During an attack, he has right-sided tearing, right eye redness and ptosis, right nasal congestion and rhinorrhea, and right-sided facial flushing.
4. He is a heavy smoker; he reports similar clusters of attacks for 3 consecutive years during autumn.
5. Between attacks he is completely symptom-free; neurological exam and brain MRI are both normal.
6. He is started on high-flow 100% oxygen via non-rebreather mask for acute attacks (10-15 L/min for 15 minutes) and sumatriptan SC; verapamil is started as preventive therapy with resolution of the cluster period after 2 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most severe primary headache; unilateral periorbital/temporal pain with ipsilateral autonomic features.
- Ipsilateral autonomic features: lacrimation, conjunctival injection, ptosis, rhinorrhea, facial flushing, Horner syndrome.
- Pattern: clusters lasting weeks to months with periods of remission; attacks at the same time daily (circadian clock involvement).
- Strongly associated with male sex, smoking.
- Acute treatment: high-flow 100% oxygen (first-line), sumatriptan SC; prevention: verapamil (first-line).

CASE_ID: 147
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hyperparathyroidism

ALIASES:
primary hyperparathyroidism
PHPT
parathyroid adenoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old woman presents for evaluation of recurrent kidney stones; she has had three calcium oxalate stones in the past 5 years and was referred after her calcium was found to be 11.4 mg/dL on routine labs.
2. She also reports fatigue, constipation, and mild depression; she denies bony pain.
3. On exam, no neck mass or lymphadenopathy is palpable; blood pressure is 148/88 mmHg.
4. Serum calcium is 11.6 mg/dL; PTH is elevated at 128 pg/mL (high-normal or elevated despite hypercalcemia); phosphorus is 2.1 mg/dL (low); 24-hour urine calcium is markedly elevated at 480 mg/day.
5. DEXA scan shows osteoporosis at the distal radius with a T-score of -2.8; X-ray of the hands shows subperiosteal bone resorption at the radial aspect of the middle phalanges.
6. Sestamibi parathyroid scan identifies a 1.4 cm right inferior parathyroid adenoma; she undergoes minimally invasive parathyroidectomy with intraoperative PTH monitoring confirming cure; calcium normalizes within 24 hours.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause of hypercalcemia in outpatients: primary hyperparathyroidism (parathyroid adenoma in 80-85%).
- Most common cause in hospitalized patients: malignancy (PTHrP or direct bone involvement).
- Classic: "bones, stones, groans, and psychic moans" (osteitis fibrosa cystica, nephrolithiasis, constipation/nausea, depression).
- Elevated PTH + high calcium + low phosphate + elevated urine calcium.
- Treatment: parathyroidectomy for symptomatic disease; cinacalcet for those who cannot undergo surgery.

CASE_ID: 148
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Aspergillosis

ALIASES:
invasive aspergillosis
Aspergillus infection
pulmonary aspergillosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man with acute myeloid leukemia in induction chemotherapy presents on day 18 of neutropenia with fever unresponsive to broad-spectrum antibacterial antibiotics, dry cough, and right-sided pleuritic chest pain.
2. He has been on fluconazole prophylaxis; his ANC has been below 100 for 14 days.
3. On exam, temperature is 39.4°C; breath sounds are decreased at the right base; he has no oral thrush.
4. Chest CT shows a 2.8 cm right upper lobe cavitating nodule with surrounding ground-glass opacity and a halo sign (ground-glass halo surrounding a nodule).
5. Serum galactomannan is markedly elevated at 3.8 (index >0.5 is positive); bronchoalveolar lavage galactomannan is also elevated and culture grows Aspergillus fumigatus.
6. He is started on voriconazole (first-line antifungal) with gradual improvement over 3 weeks; antifungal prophylaxis with posaconazole is planned after remission induction.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Invasive aspergillosis occurs in profoundly immunocompromised patients (prolonged neutropenia, HSCT, high-dose steroids).
- CT: halo sign (ground-glass surrounding nodule from hemorrhagic infarction) is classic early finding; air crescent sign appears later as neutrophils recover.
- Serum galactomannan (Aspergillus cell wall antigen): sensitive screening/diagnostic test in high-risk patients.
- Aspergillus fumigatus is most common species; septate hyphae with 45-degree angle branching on biopsy.
- Treatment: voriconazole (first-line); isavuconazole or liposomal amphotericin B as alternatives.

CASE_ID: 149
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Molar Pregnancy

ALIASES:
hydatidiform mole
gestational trophoblastic disease
complete mole
partial mole

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old woman at 12 weeks of pregnancy presents with vaginal bleeding, severe nausea and vomiting, and passage of grape-like tissue from her vagina.
2. She also reports palpitations and heat intolerance; fundal height is larger than expected for 12 weeks.
3. On exam, uterine size is consistent with 18 weeks; blood pressure is 148/92 mmHg; no fetal heart tones are heard.
4. Serum beta-hCG is markedly elevated at 580,000 mIU/mL; free T4 is elevated and TSH is suppressed (hCG cross-reacts with TSH receptor).
5. Pelvic ultrasound shows a uterine cavity filled with a classic "snowstorm" appearance without an identifiable fetus or gestational sac.
6. She undergoes suction curettage; pathology shows hydropic villi with circumferential trophoblastic proliferation and no fetal tissue — consistent with complete hydatidiform mole; serial beta-hCG monitoring is initiated and normalizes over 12 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Complete mole: 46XX karyotype (two paternal sets, no maternal DNA); no fetal tissue; high risk of gestational trophoblastic neoplasia.
- Partial mole: 69XXX or 69XXY (triploid); fetal tissue present; lower malignancy risk.
- Classic: uterus large for dates, hyperemesis, preeclampsia before 20 weeks, no fetal heart tones, "snowstorm" on US, extremely high beta-hCG.
- Hyperthyroidism from beta-hCG cross-reactivity with TSH receptor.
- Treatment: suction curettage; serial beta-hCG monitoring; chemotherapy (methotrexate) if hCG rises or plateaus.

CASE_ID: 150
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology_oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Thrombotic Thrombocytopenic Purpura

ALIASES:
TTP
thrombotic microangiopathy
ADAMTS13 deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with a 4-day history of confusion, fever, and fatigue; she was found wandering in her neighborhood by a neighbor.
2. She reports dark urine; her husband notes her skin appears yellowish and she has had headaches for a week.
3. On exam, she has jaundice, scattered petechiae on her legs, temperature 38.4°C, blood pressure 162/98 mmHg, and she is confused and cannot state the date.
4. Labs show hemoglobin 7.2 g/dL, platelets 14,000, LDH 2,200 U/L, total bilirubin 4.8 mg/dL, haptoglobin undetectable, and creatinine 1.6 mg/dL; peripheral smear shows numerous schistocytes.
5. Direct Coombs test is negative, ruling out autoimmune hemolytic anemia; ADAMTS13 activity returns at 3% with a high-titer inhibitor, confirming acquired TTP.
6. She is started on emergency plasma exchange immediately upon clinical diagnosis (before ADAMTS13 result returns); rituximab is added on day 3; by day 10 her platelet count is 180,000 and she is neurologically intact.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Pentad: MAHA + thrombocytopenia + renal failure + neurological symptoms + fever (FAT RN).
- ADAMTS13 deficiency (<10%): large vWF multimers → platelet microthrombi throughout microvasculature.
- Schistocytes on smear + low platelets + elevated LDH + negative Coombs = TTP until proven otherwise.
- Do NOT transfuse platelets (worsens thrombosis).
- Treatment: immediate plasma exchange (most critical intervention); rituximab for acquired TTP with inhibitor.

CASE_ID: 151
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Patent Ductus Arteriosus

ALIASES:
PDA
patent ductus

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A premature female infant born at 28 weeks gestation is noted on day 3 of life to have worsening respiratory distress and increasing oxygen requirements despite surfactant therapy.
2. Her nurses report bounding peripheral pulses and a hyperdynamic precordium on exam.
3. On auscultation, a continuous machine-like murmur is heard best at the left infraclavicular area, present throughout systole and diastole.
4. Chest X-ray shows cardiomegaly and increased pulmonary vascular markings with pulmonary edema.
5. Echocardiography confirms a large patent ductus arteriosus with left-to-right shunting and a dilated left atrium and left ventricle.
6. She is treated with indomethacin (a prostaglandin synthesis inhibitor) with closure of the PDA confirmed on repeat echocardiography 48 hours later and subsequent improvement in respiratory status.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- PDA: failure of ductus arteriosus to close after birth; maintained open by prostaglandins (PGE2).
- Most common in premature infants; also associated with congenital rubella syndrome.
- Continuous machine-like murmur at left infraclavicular area; bounding pulses; wide pulse pressure.
- Left-to-right shunt leads to pulmonary overcirculation and eventually Eisenmenger syndrome if uncorrected.
- Treatment: indomethacin or ibuprofen (inhibit prostaglandins); surgical ligation if medical therapy fails; maintain patency with prostaglandin E1 in duct-dependent lesions.

==================================================

CASE_ID: 152
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hypersensitivity Pneumonitis

ALIASES:
extrinsic allergic alveolitis
farmer's lung
bird fancier's lung
HP

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old farmer presents with recurrent episodes of fever, chills, myalgias, dry cough, and dyspnea occurring 4-6 hours after working in his hay barn, with improvement when away from the farm over weekends.
2. He has had three similar episodes over the past 6 months, each resolving within 24-48 hours of leaving the farm.
3. On exam during an acute episode, he is febrile at 38.8°C with fine bibasilar crackles; no wheezing is present.
4. Pulmonary function tests show a restrictive pattern with reduced DLCO; CBC shows leukocytosis without eosinophilia during acute episodes.
5. High-resolution CT shows bilateral ground-glass opacities with centrilobular nodules and a mosaic attenuation pattern predominantly in the upper and middle lobes.
6. Serum precipitins (IgG antibodies) against thermophilic Actinomyces species found in moldy hay are strongly positive; he is diagnosed with farmer's lung and advised to avoid the barn and wear a respirator.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Type III (immune complex) and Type IV (cell-mediated) hypersensitivity reaction to inhaled organic antigens.
- Common antigens: thermophilic actinomycetes in moldy hay (farmer's lung), avian proteins (bird fancier's lung), Aspergillus (malt worker's lung).
- Acute: flu-like illness 4-6 hours after exposure; resolves with antigen avoidance.
- Chronic: progressive fibrosis from repeated exposure; may be irreversible.
- Serum precipitins support diagnosis; no peripheral eosinophilia (distinguishes from eosinophilic pneumonia).

CASE_ID: 153
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Irritable Bowel Syndrome

ALIASES:
IBS
functional bowel disorder
spastic colon

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with a 2-year history of recurrent crampy lower abdominal pain, bloating, and alternating constipation and diarrhea.
2. The pain is relieved by defecation and is associated with a change in stool frequency and consistency; symptoms worsen significantly with stress and during menstruation.
3. She has no nocturnal symptoms, no blood in the stool, and no unintentional weight loss; her mother has similar symptoms.
4. On exam, mild left lower quadrant tenderness is present; no masses, organomegaly, or peritoneal signs are found.
5. CBC, CMP, TSH, CRP, fecal calprotectin, and celiac serology are all normal or negative; colonoscopy shows no mucosal abnormality.
6. She meets Rome IV criteria for IBS with mixed bowel habits (IBS-M); she is started on a low-FODMAP diet with a referral for cognitive behavioral therapy and prescribed peppermint oil capsules.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Functional bowel disorder; Rome IV criteria: recurrent abdominal pain >1 day/week for 3 months associated with ≥2 of: relation to defecation, change in stool frequency, change in stool form.
- No alarm features (bleeding, weight loss, nocturnal symptoms, age >50, family history of colon cancer/IBD) — exclude organic disease.
- Subtypes: IBS-C (constipation), IBS-D (diarrhea), IBS-M (mixed).
- Treatment: low-FODMAP diet, fiber, antispasmodics; IBS-D: loperamide, rifaximin, alosetron; IBS-C: lubiprostone, linaclotide; CBT for psychosocial component.

CASE_ID: 154
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Spontaneous Bacterial Peritonitis

ALIASES:
SBP
bacterial peritonitis
ascitic fluid infection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 54-year-old man with alcoholic cirrhosis and known ascites presents with fever, diffuse abdominal pain, and increasing confusion over 24 hours.
2. He had been stable at home but his wife reports he suddenly became more confused and less responsive yesterday evening.
3. On exam, temperature is 38.9°C, he is confused (worsened from baseline), and diffuse abdominal tenderness is present; there is no peritoneal rigidity.
4. Diagnostic paracentesis is performed immediately; ascitic fluid analysis shows 680 WBCs with 82% neutrophils (PMN count >250 cells/mm3 is diagnostic).
5. Ascitic fluid Gram stain shows no organisms; culture is sent but empiric treatment is started immediately given the PMN count.
6. Blood and ascitic fluid cultures grow E. coli; he is treated with IV cefotaxime for 5 days with clinical improvement; albumin infusion is given on days 1 and 3 to prevent hepatorenal syndrome; he is discharged on prophylactic norfloxacin.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Bacterial infection of ascitic fluid without an intra-abdominal source; occurs in decompensated cirrhosis.
- Diagnosis: ascitic fluid PMN count ≥250 cells/mm3 (treat empirically even before culture results).
- Most common organisms: E. coli, Klebsiella, Streptococcus pneumoniae (gram-negative enteric bacteria).
- Treatment: third-generation cephalosporin (cefotaxime); albumin IV reduces risk of hepatorenal syndrome.
- Secondary prophylaxis: norfloxacin or TMP-SMX indefinitely after first episode.

CASE_ID: 155
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Wernicke Encephalopathy

ALIASES:
Wernicke's encephalopathy
thiamine deficiency encephalopathy
Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 46-year-old man with a 20-year history of heavy alcohol dependence is brought to the ED by his roommate after being found confused and unable to walk.
2. He has barely eaten in 3 weeks and has been drinking continuously; his roommate reports his eyes have looked "funny" for the past 2 days.
3. On exam, he is disoriented to time and place, has horizontal nystagmus and bilateral lateral rectus palsy (CN VI), and cannot walk without falling (truncal ataxia).
4. A nurse administers IV dextrose to treat a glucose of 62 mg/dL; within 30 minutes his confusion dramatically worsens.
5. Serum thiamine level returns critically low; MRI of the brain shows symmetric T2/FLAIR signal changes in the mammillary bodies, periaqueductal gray matter, and medial thalami.
6. IV thiamine 500 mg three times daily is started immediately; his ophthalmoplegia resolves over 48 hours but significant memory impairment and confabulation persist consistent with Korsakoff syndrome.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Classic triad: confusion/encephalopathy, ophthalmoplegia (CN VI palsy, nystagmus), ataxia.
- ALWAYS give thiamine BEFORE glucose in malnourished or alcoholic patients — glucose depletes remaining thiamine and can precipitate Wernicke.
- MRI: mammillary bodies and periaqueductal gray are characteristic locations.
- Korsakoff syndrome: irreversible amnestic disorder after untreated Wernicke; anterograde amnesia + confabulation.
- Treatment: high-dose IV thiamine immediately; do not rely on oral thiamine in acute setting.

CASE_ID: 156
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pseudotumor Cerebri

ALIASES:
idiopathic intracranial hypertension
IIH
benign intracranial hypertension

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old obese woman presents with a 3-month history of daily throbbing headaches that are worse in the morning and with Valsalva, along with transient visual obscurations lasting seconds when she bends over.
2. She also reports pulsatile tinnitus that is synchronous with her heartbeat; she recently started taking oral tetracycline for acne.
3. On exam, BMI is 36; fundoscopy reveals bilateral papilledema with blurred disc margins and peripapillary hemorrhages; visual acuity is mildly reduced in both eyes.
4. MRI brain with gadolinium is normal with no mass lesion, hydrocephalus, or venous sinus thrombosis; MR venography is also normal.
5. Lumbar puncture shows an elevated opening pressure of 38 cm H2O with normal CSF composition (normal cells, protein, and glucose).
6. Tetracycline is discontinued; she is started on acetazolamide and referred for serial visual field testing; given progressive visual field loss despite medication, optic nerve sheath fenestration is performed.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Elevated ICP without a mass lesion, hydrocephalus, or identifiable cause; normal CSF composition.
- Predominantly affects obese women of childbearing age.
- Triggers: obesity, tetracyclines, vitamin A/retinoids, oral contraceptives, corticosteroid withdrawal, growth hormone.
- Classic: headache + pulsatile tinnitus + transient visual obscurations + papilledema; empty sella on MRI.
- Treatment: acetazolamide (reduces CSF production); weight loss; serial visual fields; optic nerve sheath fenestration or LP shunting for refractory disease.

CASE_ID: 157
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Goodpasture Syndrome

ALIASES:
anti-GBM disease
anti-glomerular basement membrane disease
pulmonary-renal syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old male smoker presents with a 3-week history of hemoptysis, dyspnea, and decreasing urine output.
2. He reports dark urine and bilateral leg swelling; he smokes one pack per day.
3. On exam, he appears pale with bilateral crackles at the lung bases; blood pressure is 158/96 mmHg.
4. Serum creatinine is 6.8 mg/dL (baseline unknown); urinalysis shows 4+ blood with RBC casts and 3+ protein.
5. Chest X-ray shows bilateral alveolar infiltrates consistent with diffuse alveolar hemorrhage; ANCA testing is negative.
6. Anti-GBM antibody titer is markedly elevated at >200 U/mL; renal biopsy shows linear IgG deposition along the glomerular basement membrane on immunofluorescence with crescentic glomerulonephritis; he is started on plasmapheresis plus cyclophosphamide and high-dose steroids.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Anti-GBM antibodies target type IV collagen in glomerular and alveolar basement membranes.
- Pulmonary-renal syndrome: diffuse alveolar hemorrhage + rapidly progressive glomerulonephritis.
- Smoking is a major risk factor for pulmonary involvement (damages alveolar basement membrane).
- Renal biopsy: linear IgG along GBM on immunofluorescence (distinguishes from ANCA vasculitis which shows pauci-immune pattern).
- Treatment: plasmapheresis (removes circulating antibodies) + cyclophosphamide + corticosteroids; dialysis if needed.

CASE_ID: 158
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology_oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma

ALIASES:
NHL
diffuse large B-cell lymphoma
DLBCL
follicular lymphoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old man presents with rapidly enlarging bilateral neck and axillary lymphadenopathy over 6 weeks accompanied by drenching night sweats and a 20-pound weight loss.
2. He also reports progressive dyspnea from a large mediastinal mass discovered on chest X-ray.
3. On exam, firm, nontender, matted lymph nodes are palpable in cervical, axillary, and inguinal regions; the spleen is enlarged.
4. LDH is markedly elevated at 1,240 U/L; CBC shows mild anemia and thrombocytopenia suggesting bone marrow involvement.
5. Excisional lymph node biopsy shows diffuse large B-cell lymphoma with CD20+, BCL6+, and MUM1+ expression; MYC and BCL2 rearrangements are detected by FISH (double-hit lymphoma).
6. PET-CT shows hypermetabolic disease in multiple nodal and extranodal sites including the bone marrow; he is started on R-CHOP chemotherapy with CNS prophylaxis given the double-hit status.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common lymphoma overall; DLBCL is most common aggressive NHL; follicular lymphoma most common indolent NHL.
- Non-Hodgkin spreads noncontiguously (vs. Hodgkin's contiguous spread); extranodal involvement common.
- Key marker: CD20 (B-cell) targeted by rituximab; DLBCL is CD20+.
- Double-hit lymphoma (MYC + BCL2 rearrangements): aggressive, poor prognosis.
- B symptoms: fever, night sweats, >10% weight loss (same significance as in Hodgkin).

CASE_ID: 159
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Syphilis

ALIASES:
Treponema pallidum
primary syphilis
secondary syphilis
tertiary syphilis
neurosyphilis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old man presents with a painless ulcer on his penis that he noticed 2 weeks ago; it has not resolved despite keeping it clean.
2. He reports having unprotected intercourse with multiple male partners over the past 3 months.
3. On exam, a single well-circumscribed, indurated, painless ulcer (chancre) with a clean base is present on the penile shaft; bilateral non-tender inguinal lymphadenopathy is present.
4. Darkfield microscopy of exudate from the ulcer base shows spirochetes with characteristic corkscrew motility.
5. RPR is reactive at a titer of 1:32; confirmatory treponemal test (FTA-ABS) is positive.
6. He is treated with a single dose of benzathine penicillin G 2.4 million units IM; he is tested for HIV, gonorrhea, and chlamydia; partner notification is initiated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary syphilis: painless chancre at inoculation site + regional lymphadenopathy; heals spontaneously.
- Secondary syphilis: disseminated — maculopapular rash involving palms and soles, condyloma lata, mucous patches, systemic symptoms (8-10 weeks after chancre).
- Tertiary syphilis: gummas, cardiovascular (aortitis, aortic regurgitation), neurosyphilis (tabes dorsalis, Argyll Robertson pupils).
- Congenital syphilis: saddle nose, saber shins, Hutchinson teeth, interstitial keratitis.
- Treatment: benzathine penicillin G (all stages); neurosyphilis requires IV aqueous penicillin G.

CASE_ID: 160
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Rabies

ALIASES:
rabies virus
lyssavirus
hydrophobia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 35-year-old man who returned from a 3-week rural trip to Southeast Asia 6 weeks ago presents with 4 days of fever, headache, and paresthesias radiating from a dog bite wound on his left calf that he received during his trip but did not seek treatment for.
2. Over 24 hours he develops severe agitation, hydrophobia (intense spasms when attempting to drink water), and aerophobia (spasms triggered by air blowing on his face).
3. On exam, he is highly agitated, confused, and has excessive salivation; he is unable to swallow without severe pharyngeal spasms.
4. Nuchal rigidity is present; CSF shows mild lymphocytic pleocytosis; MRI of the brain shows T2 signal changes in the brainstem and hippocampus.
5. Direct fluorescent antibody testing of a nuchal skin biopsy shows rabies antigen in nerve fibers around hair follicles; saliva RT-PCR is positive for rabies virus RNA.
6. Despite intensive supportive care using the Milwaukee protocol, he deteriorates and dies on day 7; the case highlights the critical importance of post-exposure prophylaxis (PEP) after any animal bite in an endemic area.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- RNA virus (lyssavirus); transmitted via saliva of infected animals (dogs, bats, raccoons, foxes).
- Incubation: weeks to months (longer with distal bites); virus travels along peripheral nerves to CNS.
- Clinical: prodrome (fever, paresthesias at bite site) → encephalitic (agitation, hydrophobia, aerophobia, hypersalivation) or paralytic form.
- Virtually 100% fatal once symptomatic; hydrophobia and aerophobia are pathognomonic.
- PEP: wound cleaning + rabies immunoglobulin (into wound) + rabies vaccine series (days 0, 3, 7, 14); must start immediately after exposure.

CASE_ID: 161
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Dermatomyositis

ALIASES:
inflammatory myopathy
dermatomyositis polymyositis
DM

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old woman presents with 3 months of progressive proximal muscle weakness making it difficult to rise from a chair, climb stairs, or raise her arms above her head.
2. She also reports a violaceous rash on her eyelids and a scaly, cracked rash over her knuckles that has been present for 2 months.
3. On exam, she has heliotrope rash (violaceous discoloration of the upper eyelids), Gottron papules (erythematous scaly plaques over the MCP and PIP joints), and 3/5 strength in proximal muscles bilaterally.
4. CK is markedly elevated at 4,800 U/L; aldolase and LDH are also elevated; ANA is positive; anti-Jo-1 antibody is positive.
5. MRI of the thighs shows diffuse muscle edema and inflammation; EMG shows a myopathic pattern with early recruitment and fibrillation potentials.
6. Muscle biopsy shows perifascicular atrophy and perivascular inflammatory infiltrates; she is started on high-dose prednisone with significant improvement; a CT of the chest, abdomen, and pelvis is ordered given the association with malignancy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Inflammatory myopathy with characteristic skin findings; proximal muscle weakness + elevated CK.
- Heliotrope rash (periorbital violaceous) and Gottron papules (knuckles) are pathognomonic for dermatomyositis.
- Anti-Jo-1 antibody: associated with interstitial lung disease, arthritis, mechanic's hands (antisynthetase syndrome).
- Dermatomyositis (NOT polymyositis) is strongly associated with underlying malignancy — screen with CT and age-appropriate cancer screening.
- Treatment: high-dose corticosteroids; steroid-sparing agents (methotrexate, azathioprine, IVIG).

CASE_ID: 162
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Septic Bursitis

ALIASES:
infected bursa
olecranon bursitis
prepatellar bursitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old plumber presents with 4 days of painful swelling over the tip of his right elbow; he recalls scraping his elbow at work several days before the swelling began.
2. He is febrile at 38.4°C; the swelling is warm, erythematous, and fluctuant; it is exquisitely tender to direct palpation.
3. On exam, range of motion of the elbow joint itself is preserved and painless, suggesting the process is extra-articular (bursitis rather than septic arthritis).
4. Bursal fluid is aspirated; it appears turbid and yellow-green with 48,000 WBCs and 90% neutrophils; glucose is low.
5. Gram stain shows gram-positive cocci in clusters; culture grows methicillin-sensitive Staphylococcus aureus.
6. He is started on IV oxacillin and the bursa is drained; after 48 hours of improvement he is switched to oral dicloxacillin to complete a 14-day course; repeated drainage is required at 5 days.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Inflammation of a bursa (fluid-filled sac near joints); olecranon and prepatellar most common sites.
- Septic bursitis: superficial trauma allows skin bacteria (S. aureus most common) to infect the bursa.
- Preserved range of motion of the underlying joint distinguishes bursitis from septic arthritis.
- Bursal fluid: turbid, WBC >2,000 (often much higher), organisms on culture.
- Treatment: aspiration + oral antibiotics for non-severe; IV antibiotics + repeated drainage or surgical bursectomy for severe/refractory.

CASE_ID: 163
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Obsessive-Compulsive Disorder

ALIASES:
OCD
obsessive compulsive

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old man presents requesting help for rituals that are consuming 4 hours of his day and making him late to work repeatedly.
2. He describes persistent, intrusive thoughts about contamination from germs that he knows are irrational but cannot dismiss; to neutralize the anxiety he washes his hands up to 80 times per day until they are raw and bleeding.
3. He also checks that the stove is off and doors are locked more than 30 times before leaving his apartment; he has missed social events because he cannot complete his rituals in time.
4. He reports the obsessions and compulsions are ego-dystonic (he does not want them and recognizes they are excessive) but he cannot stop.
5. Thyroid function tests, toxicology, and neurological exam are all normal; he screens negative for psychosis.
6. He is started on fluvoxamine (SSRI) at a high dose and referred for exposure and response prevention therapy (ERP); at 3 months he reports 60% reduction in ritual time.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Obsessions: recurrent, intrusive, ego-dystonic thoughts causing anxiety; compulsions: repetitive behaviors to reduce obsessional anxiety.
- Ego-dystonic (unwanted, recognized as irrational) distinguishes OCD from OCD personality disorder (ego-syntonic).
- Common themes: contamination/cleaning, symmetry/ordering, harm, forbidden thoughts.
- Treatment: SSRIs at high doses (fluvoxamine, fluxetine, sertraline) + ERP (exposure and response prevention therapy); combination is most effective.
- Clomipramine (TCA) is effective but used as second-line due to side effects.

CASE_ID: 164
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hyperosmolar Hyperglycemic State

ALIASES:
HHS
hyperosmolar nonketotic coma
HHNK
hyperglycemic hyperosmolar nonketotic syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 82-year-old woman with type 2 diabetes managed with metformin is brought to the ED by her family after 5 days of decreased oral intake, progressive confusion, and very little urination.
2. She had a urinary tract infection last week for which she was prescribed an antibiotic; she has been too unwell to drink adequately.
3. On exam, she is profoundly obtunded, severely dehydrated with sunken eyes and parched mucous membranes, and has a heart rate of 126 bpm; no fruity breath odor is present.
4. Blood glucose is 1,140 mg/dL; serum sodium is 148 mEq/L; serum osmolality is 388 mOsm/kg.
5. Arterial blood gas shows pH 7.36, bicarbonate 24 mEq/L — no significant acidosis; urine and serum ketones are trace or absent.
6. She is given aggressive IV normal saline resuscitation followed by a low-dose insulin infusion; glucose is corrected slowly over 24-36 hours; the underlying UTI is treated; she recovers neurologically over 48 hours.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Extreme hyperglycemia + hyperosmolality + severe dehydration WITHOUT significant ketoacidosis; occurs in type 2 DM.
- Enough residual insulin to prevent ketogenesis but not enough to prevent hyperglycemia.
- Serum osmolality >320 mOsm/kg; glucose often >600-1000 mg/dL; pH normal or near-normal.
- Precipitants: infection, stroke, MI, medications (steroids, thiazides), inadequate fluid intake.
- Treatment: aggressive IV fluid replacement (priority), gradual glucose lowering, electrolyte replacement; mortality higher than DKA.

CASE_ID: 165
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Gestational Diabetes

ALIASES:
GDM
gestational diabetes mellitus
pregnancy diabetes

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old obese woman at 26 weeks of her second pregnancy is referred after a routine 1-hour glucose challenge test returned a value of 188 mg/dL.
2. She had a macrosomic baby (4.6 kg) in her first pregnancy; her mother has type 2 diabetes.
3. She is asymptomatic and has no polyuria or polydipsia; fetal growth on ultrasound appears appropriate for gestational age so far.
4. A 3-hour 100g oral glucose tolerance test is performed; two of four values are abnormal (fasting 98, 1-hour 194, 2-hour 168, 3-hour 152 mg/dL), confirming gestational diabetes.
5. Fetal ultrasound at 32 weeks shows macrosomia with an estimated fetal weight in the 94th percentile; polyhydramnios is also noted.
6. She is managed with dietary modification and glucose monitoring; her fasting and postprandial values remain above target and insulin is started; she delivers at 38 weeks via cesarean due to macrosomia; postpartum glucose testing at 6 weeks shows normal glucose tolerance.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Insulin resistance developing during pregnancy; universal screening at 24-28 weeks with 1-hour GCT.
- Maternal complications: preeclampsia, cesarean delivery, future type 2 DM (50% lifetime risk).
- Fetal complications: macrosomia (shoulder dystocia risk), neonatal hypoglycemia, respiratory distress syndrome, polycythemia, jaundice, stillbirth.
- Treatment: diet and exercise first; insulin if glucose targets not met (metformin/glyburide may be used but insulin preferred).
- Hyperglycemia → fetal hyperinsulinemia → macrosomia and neonatal hypoglycemia.

CASE_ID: 166
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Respiratory Syncytial Virus Bronchiolitis

ALIASES:
RSV bronchiolitis
bronchiolitis
RSV

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-week-old male infant is brought to the ED in January with 2 days of nasal congestion, low-grade fever, and worsening difficulty breathing; his older sibling had a cold last week.
2. He has been feeding poorly, taking less than half his usual amount, and his parents note he is working very hard to breathe.
3. On exam, respiratory rate is 68/min, oxygen saturation is 88% on room air; he has nasal flaring, subcostal and intercostal retractions, and diffuse expiratory wheezes and crackles bilaterally.
4. Chest X-ray shows hyperinflation with flattened diaphragms and bilateral peribronchial thickening; no focal consolidation is seen.
5. Nasopharyngeal RSV antigen testing is positive.
6. He is admitted for supplemental oxygen, IV fluids given poor feeding, and nasal suctioning; he improves over 5 days and is discharged home; his parents are counseled on palivizumab prophylaxis for future RSV seasons given his young age.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause of bronchiolitis and infant hospitalization in winter months; RSV is most common pathogen.
- Peak age: <2 years; most severe in infants <6 months, premature infants, those with CHD or CLD.
- CXR: hyperinflation, peribronchial thickening; no focal consolidation.
- Treatment is supportive: oxygen, IV fluids, nasal suctioning; bronchodilators and steroids are NOT recommended.
- Palivizumab (RSV monoclonal antibody): prophylaxis for high-risk infants (premature, CHD, CLD).

CASE_ID: 167
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Opioid Overdose

ALIASES:
opiate overdose
narcotic overdose
heroin overdose
opioid toxidrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old man is found unresponsive in a public restroom by emergency services; several used syringes and a small bag of white powder are nearby.
2. He has no known medical history; bystanders report he was last seen awake approximately 45 minutes ago.
3. On exam, he is unresponsive with a GCS of 4; respiratory rate is 4 breaths per minute; oxygen saturation is 68% on room air.
4. Both pupils are pinpoint at 1 mm bilaterally and do not react to light; he has diffuse muscle flaccidity and track marks on bilateral antecubital fossae.
5. Blood glucose is 88 mg/dL; within 90 seconds of IV naloxone 0.4 mg administration, he opens his eyes, begins breathing spontaneously, and his oxygen saturation rises to 96%.
6. He is observed for 4 hours given the risk of re-sedation as naloxone wears off before the opioid; fentanyl and heroin are detected on confirmatory urine toxicology; he is offered medication-assisted treatment with buprenorphine before discharge.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Classic opioid toxidrome: CNS depression, respiratory depression, miosis (pinpoint pupils) — the triad.
- Naloxone: competitive mu-opioid receptor antagonist; short duration (30-90 min) — re-sedation risk with long-acting opioids.
- Fentanyl analogs may require higher or repeated naloxone doses.
- Heroin, morphine, oxycodone, methadone (very long-acting), fentanyl.
- Treatment: airway support + naloxone; consider intubation if no response; MAT (methadone or buprenorphine) for opioid use disorder.

CASE_ID: 168
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Methanol Toxicity

ALIASES:
methanol poisoning
wood alcohol toxicity
toxic alcohol ingestion

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 44-year-old man with alcohol use disorder presents to the ED 12 hours after drinking an unknown clear liquid from an unlabeled bottle at a party when his usual alcohol supply ran out.
2. He initially felt intoxicated but improved; now he has severe headache, nausea, vomiting, and his vision has become progressively blurry over the past 4 hours.
3. On exam, visual acuity is markedly reduced bilaterally; fundoscopy shows bilateral optic disc hyperemia and edema; he is confused.
4. ABG shows pH 7.08, bicarbonate 8 mEq/L, and PaCO2 18 mmHg (compensatory); calculated anion gap is 32 mEq/L; osmol gap is elevated at 22 mOsm/kg.
5. Serum methanol level is 48 mg/dL; formate level is elevated; ethanol level is undetectable.
6. He is given IV fomepizole (alcohol dehydrogenase inhibitor) immediately along with sodium bicarbonate; hemodialysis is initiated given his methanol level, severe metabolic acidosis, and visual impairment; despite treatment, he retains significant visual impairment.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Methanol metabolized by alcohol dehydrogenase → formaldehyde → formate (toxic metabolite causing optic nerve and CNS toxicity).
- Classic: initial mild intoxication → latent period → severe anion gap metabolic acidosis + visual disturbance (snowfield vision → blindness).
- High anion gap + high osmol gap; late presentation when ethanol is undetectable (ethanol is protective by competing for alcohol dehydrogenase).
- Treatment: fomepizole (alcohol dehydrogenase inhibitor, preferred) or ethanol; bicarbonate; hemodialysis for severe cases.
- Ethylene glycol: similar presentation but targets kidneys (calcium oxalate crystals in urine) rather than eyes.
