CASE_ID: 169
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Hemorrhoids

ALIASES:
internal hemorrhoids
external hemorrhoids
hemorrhoidal disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old man presents with bright red blood on the toilet paper and dripping into the toilet bowl after bowel movements for the past 3 months; he has no pain with defecation.
2. He reports a low-fiber diet and spends extended time on the toilet; he has noticed a soft tissue protrusion from his anus after straining that reduces spontaneously.
3. On exam, external inspection shows no external hemorrhoids at rest; digital rectal exam is nontender; anoscopy reveals large internal hemorrhoids at the 3, 7, and 11 o'clock positions with prolapse to the anal verge during straining (grade III).
4. Stool guaiac testing is positive; given his age and rectal bleeding, flexible sigmoidoscopy is performed and shows no polyps or mucosal lesions beyond the hemorrhoids.
5. He is started on increased dietary fiber, adequate hydration, and sitz baths; he is referred for rubber band ligation of the internal hemorrhoids.
6. After rubber band ligation of all three hemorrhoidal columns over three sessions, bleeding and prolapse resolve completely; he is counseled on fiber and fluid maintenance.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Internal hemorrhoids: above dentate line; painless bright red rectal bleeding; graded I-IV by degree of prolapse.
- External hemorrhoids: below dentate line; painful (somatic innervation); risk of thrombosis.
- Thrombosed external hemorrhoid: acute onset severe perianal pain with a tender, firm, bluish lump — excision within 72 hours.
- Treatment: fiber + fluids + sitz baths for mild; rubber band ligation for grade I-III internal; surgery for grade IV or failed banding.
- Always rule out colorectal cancer in patients over 45 or with alarm features.

==================================================

CASE_ID: 170
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Rosacea

ALIASES:
acne rosacea
rosacea vulgaris

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 44-year-old fair-skinned woman of Irish descent presents with a 5-year history of facial flushing, persistent redness, and pimple-like bumps on her cheeks and nose that have progressively worsened.
2. She reports episodes of intense flushing triggered by hot beverages, alcohol, spicy foods, sun exposure, and emotional stress.
3. On exam, there is persistent centrofacial erythema with telangiectasias on both cheeks and nose; inflammatory papules and pustules are present but no comedones (distinguishing it from acne).
4. Her eyes are red and irritated with crusting of the eyelid margins; she reports a gritty foreign-body sensation in both eyes (ocular rosacea).
5. Skin biopsy shows perivascular and perifollicular lymphocytic infiltration with Demodex mites in hair follicles.
6. She is started on topical metronidazole for the facial erythema and papules, oral doxycycline for the ocular component, and counseled on trigger avoidance; pulsed dye laser therapy is planned for the persistent telangiectasias.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Chronic inflammatory skin condition affecting the central face of fair-skinned individuals.
- Four subtypes: erythematotelangiectatic (flushing, redness), papulopustular (papules/pustules without comedones), phymatous (rhinophyma — bulbous nose), ocular.
- Key distinguishing feature from acne: NO comedones in rosacea.
- Triggers: heat, alcohol, spicy food, sun, exercise, emotional stress, Demodex mites.
- Treatment: topical metronidazole/azelaic acid/ivermectin; oral doxycycline for papulopustular; laser for telangiectasias; avoid triggers.

CASE_ID: 171
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Unstable Angina

ALIASES:
UA
acute coronary syndrome without infarction
rest angina

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man with hypertension and hyperlipidemia presents with chest pain that has been occurring at rest for the past 6 hours, each episode lasting 20 minutes.
2. He had exertional chest tightness for the past 2 weeks that he dismissed as heartburn, but tonight the pain woke him from sleep.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 154/92 mmHg; he appears diaphoretic and anxious; lungs are clear.
4. ECG shows new ST depression of 1.5 mm in leads V4-V6 and T-wave inversions in the lateral leads without ST elevation.
5. Serial troponins at 0 and 3 hours are both undetectable, ruling out myocardial infarction.
6. He is started on aspirin, heparin, and high-intensity statin; coronary angiography reveals 85% stenosis of the LAD and he undergoes PCI with drug-eluting stent placement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Unstable angina: new-onset angina, angina at rest, or crescendo angina WITHOUT troponin elevation.
- Distinguished from NSTEMI: both have ST depression/T-wave changes but NSTEMI has elevated troponin.
- Part of the ACS spectrum: UA and NSTEMI are clinically managed identically before troponin results.
- Treatment: dual antiplatelet therapy (aspirin + P2Y12 inhibitor), anticoagulation, statin, beta-blocker; early invasive strategy for high-risk features.

CASE_ID: 172
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Dilated Cardiomyopathy

ALIASES:
DCM
congestive cardiomyopathy
idiopathic cardiomyopathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old man presents with progressive dyspnea on exertion, orthopnea, and bilateral leg swelling developing over 4 months.
2. He drinks alcohol heavily and had a viral illness with chest pain 6 months ago that resolved on its own.
3. On exam, JVP is elevated, bibasilar crackles are present, an S3 gallop is audible, and bilateral pitting edema extends to the knees.
4. ECG shows sinus tachycardia with left bundle branch block; chest X-ray shows cardiomegaly with pulmonary vascular congestion and Kerley B lines.
5. Echocardiography shows a severely dilated left ventricle with an ejection fraction of 18% and global hypokinesis without regional wall motion abnormalities.
6. Cardiac MRI shows mid-wall fibrosis in a non-ischemic pattern; coronary angiography is normal; he is started on guideline-directed medical therapy and referred for ICD evaluation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cardiomyopathy; four-chamber dilation with systolic dysfunction.
- Causes: idiopathic (most common), viral myocarditis, alcohol, peripartum, doxorubicin, Chagas disease, hemochromatosis.
- Global hypokinesis distinguishes from ischemic cardiomyopathy which causes regional wall motion abnormalities.
- Treatment: ACEi/ARB/ARNi, beta-blocker, MRA, SGLT2i; ICD for EF <35%; cardiac transplant for refractory disease.

CASE_ID: 173
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology

DIAGNOSIS:
Atrial Flutter

ALIASES:
AFL
typical atrial flutter
cavotricuspid isthmus flutter

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man with COPD presents with palpitations and mild dyspnea that began abruptly 8 hours ago; he has no prior arrhythmia history.
2. He reports a similar episode 6 months ago that resolved on its own after a few hours.
3. On exam, heart rate is 150 bpm and regular; blood pressure is 118/76 mmHg; lungs have mild expiratory wheezes.
4. ECG shows a regular narrow-complex tachycardia at 150 bpm with sawtooth flutter waves best seen in leads II, III, and aVF at a rate of 300 bpm with 2:1 AV conduction.
5. Carotid sinus massage transiently slows the ventricular rate revealing the underlying flutter waves more clearly without terminating the arrhythmia.
6. He is electrically cardioverted to sinus rhythm and started on anticoagulation; cavotricuspid isthmus ablation is planned for definitive treatment given his recurrent episodes.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Atrial rate typically 300 bpm; ventricular rate depends on AV conduction ratio (2:1 most common = 150 bpm ventricular rate).
- Classic: regular narrow-complex tachycardia at exactly 150 bpm; sawtooth flutter waves best seen in inferior leads.
- Carotid sinus massage or adenosine transiently increases AV block revealing flutter waves but does not terminate the arrhythmia.
- Cavotricuspid isthmus-dependent (typical) flutter is highly amenable to catheter ablation with >95% cure rate.
- Anticoagulation risk is the same as AFib; treat with the same stroke prevention approach.

CASE_ID: 174
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pulmonology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pulmonary Arterial Hypertension

ALIASES:
PAH
pulmonary hypertension
primary pulmonary hypertension

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old woman presents with a 2-year history of progressive exertional dyspnea, fatigue, and near-syncope with exertion that has worsened significantly over the past 3 months.
2. She has no history of cardiac or pulmonary disease and no risk factors for thromboembolic disease; she does not use IV drugs.
3. On exam, a loud P2, right ventricular heave, tricuspid regurgitation murmur, elevated JVP, and peripheral edema are all present.
4. ECG shows right axis deviation and right ventricular hypertrophy; chest X-ray shows enlarged central pulmonary arteries with peripheral pruning.
5. Echocardiography estimates pulmonary artery systolic pressure of 78 mmHg with severe right ventricular dilation and dysfunction.
6. Right heart catheterization confirms mean pulmonary artery pressure of 52 mmHg with pulmonary vascular resistance of 12 Wood units and a normal wedge pressure, confirming group 1 PAH; she is started on combination ambrisentan plus tadalafil.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Defined as mean PAP >20 mmHg on right heart catheterization with normal wedge pressure (<15 mmHg); gold standard for diagnosis.
- Group 1 PAH: idiopathic, heritable, drug-induced (anorexigens, methamphetamine), connective tissue disease, HIV, portal hypertension.
- Clinical signs of right heart failure: loud P2, RV heave, TR murmur, elevated JVP, peripheral edema.
- Treatment (Group 1): prostacyclins (epoprostenol), endothelin receptor antagonists (ambrisentan/bosentan), PDE-5 inhibitors (sildenafil/tadalafil); combination therapy preferred.
- Poor prognosis without treatment; median survival historically 2.8 years; modern therapy has significantly improved outcomes.

CASE_ID: 175
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Liver Failure

ALIASES:
fulminant hepatic failure
ALF
acute hepatic failure

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old woman is brought to the ED confused and jaundiced 5 days after ingesting 40 acetaminophen tablets in a suicide attempt she did not disclose to anyone.
2. She is disoriented and unable to state the date; her family reports she has been increasingly confused over 2 days with no prior liver disease.
3. On exam, she is deeply jaundiced with asterixis, fetor hepaticus, and right upper quadrant tenderness; there are no stigmata of chronic liver disease.
4. ALT is 8,400 U/L, AST is 6,200 U/L, total bilirubin 18 mg/dL, INR 6.8, and creatinine 3.2 mg/dL; blood glucose is 54 mg/dL.
5. Ammonia is 148 mcmol/L; CT head shows no intracranial pathology; she is transferred to the ICU.
6. N-acetylcysteine infusion is started immediately; she meets King's College Criteria for emergent listing and undergoes successful liver transplantation on day 3 with full recovery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute liver failure: encephalopathy + coagulopathy (INR >1.5) in a patient without pre-existing liver disease within 26 weeks.
- Most common cause in the US: acetaminophen (50%); also hepatitis B, autoimmune, Wilson disease, Budd-Chiari, drug-induced.
- Complications: encephalopathy, cerebral edema (leading cause of death), coagulopathy, hypoglycemia, renal failure, infection.
- N-acetylcysteine is beneficial even in non-acetaminophen ALF.
- King's College Criteria determines need for emergent liver transplant listing.

CASE_ID: 176
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Ischemic Colitis

ALIASES:
colonic ischemia
mesenteric venous thrombosis colitis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old woman with hypertension, atrial fibrillation, and peripheral vascular disease presents with sudden onset left-sided abdominal cramping and bloody diarrhea beginning 4 hours ago.
2. She underwent elective aortic aneurysm repair 3 days ago and was recovering well until this episode began.
3. On exam, she has left-sided abdominal tenderness without peritoneal signs; temperature is 38.1°C; blood pressure is stable.
4. WBC is 16,200; lactate is 2.8 mmol/L; CT abdomen shows circumferential wall thickening of the sigmoid colon with pericolonic fat stranding and thumbprinting.
5. Flexible sigmoidoscopy shows patchy mucosal pallor, submucosal hemorrhage, and superficial ulcerations with a sharp transition to normal mucosa.
6. She is managed with bowel rest, IV fluids, and broad-spectrum antibiotics; repeat sigmoidoscopy at 72 hours shows improvement and she recovers without surgery.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common form of intestinal ischemia; watershed areas most vulnerable: splenic flexure and rectosigmoid junction.
- Risk factors: aortic surgery (interrupts IMA), low-flow states, atherosclerosis, hypercoagulable states, constipation-inducing medications.
- Sudden left-sided abdominal pain + bloody diarrhea in an elderly patient = ischemic colitis until proven otherwise.
- CT: thumbprinting (submucosal edema); colonoscopy is the diagnostic gold standard.
- Usually self-limited; surgery for peritonitis, gangrene, or failure to improve within 48-72 hours.

CASE_ID: 177
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Insulinoma

ALIASES:
pancreatic beta cell tumor
insulin-secreting tumor
islet cell tumor

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old woman presents with 8 months of recurrent episodes of sweating, tremulousness, palpitations, and confusion occurring specifically before breakfast and whenever she skips meals.
2. Her husband reports she has had two episodes of loss of consciousness, both in the early morning hours before eating.
3. Blood glucose during a symptomatic episode is 38 mg/dL; symptoms resolve completely within minutes of eating.
4. A 72-hour supervised fast is performed; at hour 14 she becomes symptomatic with glucose 34 mg/dL, insulin 48 mU/L (inappropriately elevated), C-peptide elevated, and proinsulin elevated; glucagon administration raises glucose by >25 mg/dL.
5. CT abdomen shows a 1.8 cm hypervascular mass in the pancreatic tail.
6. Laparoscopic distal pancreatectomy is performed; pathology confirms a well-differentiated insulinoma; blood glucose normalizes immediately postoperatively and she has no further hypoglycemic episodes.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common pancreatic islet cell tumor; benign in 90%; associated with MEN1.
- Whipple triad: symptoms of hypoglycemia + low plasma glucose during symptoms + relief with glucose.
- 72-hour fast is the gold standard provocative test; demonstrates inappropriate insulin secretion during hypoglycemia.
- Elevated C-peptide distinguishes from exogenous insulin administration (which suppresses C-peptide).
- Treatment: surgical resection is curative; diazoxide for unresectable disease.

CASE_ID: 178
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Thyroid Cancer

ALIASES:
papillary thyroid carcinoma
PTC
thyroid carcinoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman presents after her primary care physician palpated a nodule in the right thyroid lobe during a routine physical; she had head and neck radiation as a child.
2. She denies dysphagia, hoarseness, or neck pain; TSH is normal.
3. On exam, a firm 2.2 cm nodule is palpable in the right thyroid lobe with a firm right cervical lymph node measuring 1.5 cm.
4. Thyroid ultrasound shows a hypoechoic nodule with microcalcifications, irregular margins, and taller-than-wide shape — all high-risk sonographic features.
5. Fine-needle aspiration biopsy shows cells with ground-glass (Orphan Annie eye) nuclei, nuclear grooves, and psammoma bodies consistent with papillary thyroid carcinoma.
6. She undergoes total thyroidectomy with central neck dissection; pathology confirms papillary thyroid carcinoma with lymph node metastasis; radioactive iodine ablation is given and suppressive levothyroxine is started.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common thyroid malignancy (85%); associated with prior radiation exposure and RET/PTC rearrangements.
- Papillary: Orphan Annie eye nuclei, nuclear grooves, psammoma bodies; spreads to lymph nodes; excellent prognosis.
- Follicular: vascular and capsular invasion; hematogenous spread to lung and bone; RAS mutation.
- Medullary: from parafollicular C cells; secretes calcitonin; associated with MEN2A and MEN2B; RET mutation.
- Anaplastic: most aggressive; rapidly fatal; undifferentiated; affects elderly patients.

CASE_ID: 179
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Peripheral Neuropathy

ALIASES:
diabetic peripheral neuropathy
distal symmetric polyneuropathy
length-dependent neuropathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man with poorly controlled type 2 diabetes (HbA1c 10.8%) presents with burning pain, numbness, and tingling in both feet that has progressively worsened over 2 years.
2. He also reports balance problems in the dark and difficulty sensing temperature when he showers.
3. On exam, vibration sense and proprioception are absent at the toes and ankles bilaterally; pinprick and light touch are diminished in a stocking distribution to mid-calf.
4. Deep tendon reflexes are absent at the ankles bilaterally; Romberg test is positive; there is no muscle weakness.
5. Nerve conduction studies show reduced amplitudes and mildly slowed velocities in bilateral sural and peroneal nerves consistent with a length-dependent axonal sensory polyneuropathy.
6. He is started on pregabalin for neuropathic pain; HbA1c is aggressively optimized; he is referred for podiatric care and custom diabetic footwear to prevent ulceration.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common complication of diabetes; length-dependent axonal neuropathy in a stocking-glove distribution.
- Sensory symptoms predominate: burning, tingling, numbness; loss of vibration and proprioception; absent ankle reflexes.
- NCS: axonal pattern (reduced amplitudes) in diabetic neuropathy vs. demyelinating (slowed velocities) in Guillain-Barré.
- Autonomic neuropathy: orthostatic hypotension, gastroparesis, bladder dysfunction, erectile dysfunction, gustatory sweating.
- Treatment: optimize glycemic control (most important); gabapentin/pregabalin, duloxetine, TCAs for neuropathic pain.

CASE_ID: 180
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Cauda Equina Syndrome

ALIASES:
CES
cauda equina compression
conus medullaris syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old man presents to the ED with 12 hours of severe low back pain radiating bilaterally to both legs, progressive leg weakness, and difficulty urinating since this morning.
2. He had a prior lumbar disc herniation but had been doing well; the current pain began after heavy lifting.
3. On exam, bilateral lower extremity weakness is present; sensation is absent in the perineum, inner thighs, and perianal area (saddle anesthesia).
4. Anal sphincter tone is markedly reduced; bladder scan shows 680 mL of retained urine; deep tendon reflexes are absent at the knees and ankles bilaterally.
5. Emergency MRI of the lumbar spine shows a massive central L4-L5 disc herniation compressing the cauda equina nerve roots bilaterally with no remaining CSF signal.
6. Emergency surgical decompression is performed within 3 hours; bladder and bowel function partially return over 6 months of rehabilitation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Surgical emergency; compression of nerve roots below L1 (cauda equina).
- Classic pentad: low back pain, bilateral leg weakness/sensory loss, saddle anesthesia, bowel dysfunction, urinary retention.
- Saddle anesthesia (perineum, inner thighs, buttocks) is pathognomonic; urinary retention is the most consistent finding.
- Most common cause: large central lumbar disc herniation; also tumor, epidural abscess, trauma.
- Treatment: emergency MRI followed by immediate surgical decompression; delay worsens prognosis for bladder and bowel recovery.

CASE_ID: 181
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Vertebral Artery Dissection

ALIASES:
cervical artery dissection
posterior circulation stroke
vertebral dissection

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old woman presents with sudden onset severe occipital headache and neck pain that began immediately after a chiropractic neck manipulation 2 hours ago.
2. She now has vertigo, nausea, vomiting, and difficulty walking; she has never had a stroke or prior neurological symptoms.
3. On exam, she has ipsilateral facial numbness, ipsilateral Horner syndrome, ipsilateral limb ataxia, contralateral body pain and temperature loss (crossed sensory deficit), and nystagmus.
4. CT head is normal; MRI of the brain shows restricted diffusion in the right lateral medulla (Wallenberg syndrome).
5. MR angiography shows a right vertebral artery dissection with an intimal flap and pseudoaneurysm formation at the C1-C2 level.
6. She is anticoagulated with heparin bridged to warfarin for 3-6 months; her vertigo and ataxia improve over weeks with residual sensory deficits.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Vertebral artery dissection: tear of the intima allowing blood to enter the vessel wall; thromboembolism causes posterior circulation stroke.
- Triggers: chiropractic neck manipulation, trauma, sudden head movement; also spontaneous (connective tissue disorders such as Ehlers-Danlos, Marfan).
- Wallenberg (lateral medullary) syndrome: ipsilateral face numbness + Horner syndrome + ataxia + dysphagia; contralateral body pain and temperature loss.
- MRA or CTA of neck vessels is diagnostic.
- Treatment: anticoagulation for 3-6 months; antiplatelet therapy is an alternative; surgical intervention for refractory or expanding dissections.

CASE_ID: 182
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lupus Nephritis

ALIASES:
SLE nephritis
diffuse proliferative lupus nephritis
class IV lupus nephritis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old African American woman with known SLE presents with worsening facial and leg edema, foamy urine, and new hypertension over 3 weeks.
2. She ran out of hydroxychloroquine 2 months ago and has not restarted it.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 162/104 mmHg; she has a butterfly rash and bilateral pedal edema.
4. Urinalysis shows 4+ protein with RBC casts; 24-hour urine protein is 4.8 g; serum creatinine has risen from 0.8 to 2.4 mg/dL over 3 weeks.
5. Complement C3 and C4 are markedly reduced; anti-dsDNA titers have risen fourfold; anti-Smith antibodies are positive.
6. Renal biopsy shows diffuse proliferative glomerulonephritis (class IV) with wire-loop lesions and full-house immunofluorescence (IgG, IgA, IgM, C3, C1q); she is started on IV methylprednisolone followed by mycophenolate mofetil and hydroxychloroquine.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most serious complication of SLE; class IV (diffuse proliferative) is the most common and severe form.
- Active lupus nephritis: hematuria + proteinuria + RBC casts + rising creatinine + low complement + rising anti-dsDNA.
- Full-house immunofluorescence (all immunoglobulins plus complement) is characteristic of lupus nephritis.
- Wire-loop lesions: large subendothelial immune deposits on light microscopy; pathognomonic for class IV.
- Treatment: class III/IV induction with high-dose steroids + mycophenolate or cyclophosphamide; maintenance with mycophenolate + hydroxychloroquine.

CASE_ID: 183
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Membranous Nephropathy

ALIASES:
membranous glomerulonephritis
idiopathic membranous nephropathy
anti-PLA2R nephropathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 52-year-old man presents with bilateral leg swelling and frothy urine developing over 6 weeks with no prior kidney disease.
2. He has a history of hepatitis B surface antigenemia monitored for 10 years; he takes no nephrotoxic medications.
3. On exam, he has significant bilateral pitting edema to the thighs and periorbital edema; blood pressure is 148/88 mmHg.
4. Urinalysis shows 4+ protein; 24-hour urine protein is 7.2 g; serum albumin is 1.6 g/dL; cholesterol is 380 mg/dL.
5. Anti-phospholipase A2 receptor (anti-PLA2R) antibody is strongly positive; complement levels are normal; ANA is negative.
6. Renal biopsy shows diffuse thickening of the GBM with subepithelial immune complex deposits and spike-and-dome pattern on electron microscopy; he is started on rituximab with gradual reduction in proteinuria.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause of nephrotic syndrome in white adults; anti-PLA2R antibody is pathognomonic for primary membranous nephropathy.
- Secondary causes: hepatitis B (most common worldwide), malignancy (especially solid tumors in older adults), SLE, drugs (NSAIDs, gold, penicillamine).
- Pathology: subepithelial immune deposits, GBM thickening (spikes on silver stain), no proliferation, spike-and-dome on EM.
- Rule of thirds: 1/3 spontaneous remission, 1/3 persistent proteinuria, 1/3 progressive renal failure.
- Treatment: conservative (ACEi/ARB) for low risk; rituximab or calcineurin inhibitors for high-risk or progressive disease.

CASE_ID: 184
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology_oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Myelodysplastic Syndrome

ALIASES:
MDS
myelodysplasia
refractory anemia with excess blasts

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old man presents for evaluation of persistent fatigue and progressive pallor over 6 months requiring two blood transfusions; he was treated for NHL 8 years ago with alkylating agents and radiation.
2. On exam, he appears pale with mild splenomegaly; no lymphadenopathy is present.
3. CBC shows hemoglobin 7.4 g/dL, WBC 2,800, platelets 68,000; peripheral smear shows hypersegmented neutrophils, pseudo-Pelger-Huet cells, and macrocytic red cells.
4. Reticulocyte count is inappropriately low at 0.4%; B12, folate, and iron studies are all normal.
5. Bone marrow biopsy shows dysplastic changes in all three cell lines with 8% blasts; cytogenetics reveal deletion 5q and monosomy 7.
6. He is started on azacitidine (hypomethylating agent) and evaluated for allogeneic stem cell transplant given his high-risk cytogenetics.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Clonal disorder of hematopoietic stem cells causing dysplastic blood cell production and cytopenias; can transform to AML (blasts >20%).
- Risk factors: prior alkylating chemotherapy, radiation, benzene exposure, advanced age.
- Peripheral smear: pseudo-Pelger-Huet cells, hypersegmented neutrophils, macrocytes, thrombocytopenia.
- IPSS-R scoring system guides prognosis and treatment decisions.
- Treatment: supportive care (transfusions, growth factors); hypomethylating agents (azacitidine, decitabine); allogeneic HSCT for eligible high-risk patients.

CASE_ID: 185
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology_oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Essential Thrombocythemia

ALIASES:
ET
essential thrombocytosis
primary thrombocythemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 55-year-old woman presents after routine bloodwork shows a platelet count of 1,240,000; she reports occasional headaches and intermittent tingling in her fingertips.
2. She has no history of infection, inflammation, iron deficiency, or splenectomy that could cause reactive thrombocytosis.
3. On exam, mild splenomegaly is palpable; no lymphadenopathy is present; the remainder of the exam is normal.
4. CBC shows isolated thrombocytosis; hemoglobin and WBC are normal; peripheral smear shows large platelets with occasional aggregates.
5. Serum EPO and iron studies are normal; bone marrow biopsy shows megakaryocytic hyperplasia with large hypersegmented megakaryocytes without significant fibrosis.
6. JAK2 V617F mutation is positive; she is classified as high-risk ET (age >60) and started on hydroxyurea plus low-dose aspirin; platelet count normalizes to 450,000 over 3 months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Myeloproliferative neoplasm with isolated marked thrombocytosis; JAK2 V617F in 50-60% (also CALR and MPL mutations).
- Major complications: thrombosis (most serious) and hemorrhage (paradoxically at very high platelet counts due to acquired von Willebrand disease).
- Distinguish from reactive thrombocytosis: reactive has normal JAK2, normal bone marrow, resolves with underlying cause treatment.
- Risk stratification: high risk (age >60, prior thrombosis, JAK2+, cardiovascular risk factors) → hydroxyurea + aspirin; low risk → aspirin alone.
- Transformation to myelofibrosis or AML occurs in a small percentage over time.

CASE_ID: 186
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Dengue Fever

ALIASES:
dengue
dengue hemorrhagic fever
breakbone fever

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old woman presents with 4 days of sudden high fever, severe headache, retro-orbital pain, and diffuse severe myalgias and arthralgias after returning from Bali, Indonesia.
2. She reports the muscle pain is so severe it feels like her bones are breaking; she has had nausea and no appetite.
3. On exam, temperature is 39.8°C; she has a morbilliform rash on her trunk; scattered petechiae are present and the tourniquet test is positive.
4. WBC is 2,400 (leukopenia), platelets are 68,000 (thrombocytopenia), and hematocrit is mildly elevated; AST and ALT are elevated.
5. Dengue NS1 antigen is positive on day 4; dengue IgM serology is positive on day 5.
6. She is managed with supportive care including IV fluids and acetaminophen; NSAIDs and aspirin are avoided due to bleeding risk; she fully recovers over 10 days.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Flavivirus transmitted by Aedes aegypti mosquito; tropical and subtropical regions; four serotypes (DENV 1-4).
- Classic triad: high fever + severe myalgias and arthralgias (breakbone fever) + rash (morbilliform with islands of white in sea of red).
- Lab findings: leukopenia, thrombocytopenia, elevated hematocrit (hemoconcentration), elevated LFTs.
- Dengue hemorrhagic fever: plasma leakage from increased vascular permeability → hemoconcentration → pleural effusions, ascites, shock.
- Avoid aspirin and NSAIDs (worsen bleeding); supportive care only; no specific antiviral treatment.

CASE_ID: 187
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Varicella Zoster Primary Infection

ALIASES:
chickenpox
varicella
primary VZV

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-year-old unvaccinated boy presents with 2 days of fever and a pruritic rash that began on his trunk and has spread to his face, scalp, and extremities.
2. Several of his classmates had a similar illness 2 weeks ago; he has been scratching intensely.
3. On exam, he has a characteristic rash showing all stages simultaneously: macules, papules, vesicles (dewdrop on a rose petal), and crusted lesions on an erythematous base on his trunk, face, and scalp.
4. The rash spares the palms and soles; lesions are present inside his mouth; several vesicles have become crusted.
5. Tzanck smear from a vesicle base shows multinucleated giant cells; direct fluorescent antibody testing confirms varicella-zoster virus.
6. He is managed supportively with antihistamines for pruritus, kept home from school, and his immunocompromised grandmother is given VZV immune globulin as post-exposure prophylaxis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Primary VZV infection; highly contagious via airborne transmission; incubation 10-21 days.
- Classic: pruritic rash in crops at all stages simultaneously (macules → papules → vesicles → crusts); dewdrop-on-rose-petal appearance.
- Complications: bacterial superinfection (most common in children), pneumonia (most serious in adults), encephalitis, Reye syndrome (if aspirin given).
- Tzanck smear: multinucleated giant cells (also positive in HSV — cannot distinguish the two viruses).
- Treatment: supportive in healthy children; acyclovir for adults, immunocompromised, neonates; varicella vaccine is live-attenuated (do not give in pregnancy or immunosuppression).

==================================================

CASE_ID: 188
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Anterior Shoulder Dislocation

ALIASES:
shoulder dislocation
glenohumeral dislocation
anterior glenohumeral dislocation

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 22-year-old football player is brought to the sideline after his right arm was forcefully abducted and externally rotated during a tackle; he is holding his arm in slight abduction and external rotation.
2. He is in significant pain and will not allow anyone to move his arm; this is his third shoulder dislocation on the same side.
3. On exam, there is a visible step-off deformity at the right shoulder with a flattened deltoid; the humeral head is palpable anteriorly below the coracoid process.
4. Neurovascular exam shows reduced sensation over the lateral deltoid (axillary nerve distribution) and weak shoulder abduction.
5. X-ray shows an anterior dislocation of the humeral head with a Hill-Sachs lesion and a Bankart lesion (fracture of the anterior-inferior glenoid rim).
6. The shoulder is reduced using the Cunningham technique with immediate pain relief; post-reduction X-ray confirms appropriate position; he is referred for arthroscopic Bankart repair given his recurrent dislocations.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common joint dislocation; anterior dislocation (95%) results from forced abduction and external rotation.
- Arm held in slight abduction and external rotation; step-off deformity; humeral head palpable anteriorly.
- Complications: axillary nerve injury (deltoid weakness, lateral deltoid numbness), rotator cuff tear, Hill-Sachs lesion, Bankart lesion.
- Hill-Sachs lesion: posterior humeral head compression fracture from impact with the glenoid rim.
- Bankart lesion: anterior-inferior glenoid labral tear; predisposes to recurrent dislocation.
- Treatment: closed reduction; immobilization; surgical stabilization for recurrent dislocations or athletes.

CASE_ID: 189
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Carpal Tunnel Syndrome

ALIASES:
CTS
median nerve entrapment
median nerve compression at wrist

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 44-year-old woman presents with 8 months of numbness, tingling, and burning pain in her right thumb, index, middle, and lateral half of her ring finger that is worst at night and wakes her from sleep.
2. She works as a data entry clerk and notices her symptoms worsen with prolonged keyboard use; she has been shaking her hand to relieve symptoms (flick sign).
3. On exam, Tinel sign is positive (tapping over the carpal tunnel at the wrist reproduces tingling); Phalen test is positive (60 seconds of wrist flexion reproduces symptoms).
4. Sensation is reduced in the lateral 3.5 fingers; thenar muscle atrophy is present on the right indicating chronic and severe compression.
5. Nerve conduction studies show prolonged distal motor latency and reduced sensory nerve action potential amplitude of the right median nerve across the wrist.
6. Given thenar atrophy indicating severe CTS, she undergoes carpal tunnel release with resolution of nocturnal symptoms and gradual return of sensation over 3 months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common peripheral nerve entrapment; median nerve compressed under the transverse carpal ligament.
- Sensory distribution: lateral 3.5 fingers (thumb, index, middle, lateral ring); the little finger is NOT affected (ulnar nerve).
- Risk factors: pregnancy, hypothyroidism, diabetes, RA, acromegaly, repetitive use, obesity.
- Tinel sign (tapping) and Phalen sign (wrist flexion 60 seconds); thenar atrophy indicates severe chronic compression.
- Treatment: wrist splinting at night (mild), corticosteroid injection (moderate), surgical carpal tunnel release (severe or refractory).

CASE_ID: 190
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Rotator Cuff Tear

ALIASES:
supraspinatus tear
rotator cuff injury
rotator cuff rupture

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old carpenter presents with a 3-month history of right shoulder pain and weakness that began after lifting a heavy box; he cannot raise his arm above shoulder height.
2. He has had intermittent right shoulder pain for years from overhead work; the current injury is significantly worse.
3. On exam, a painful arc of motion is present between 60-120 degrees of abduction; he cannot resist downward pressure when his arm is abducted to 90 degrees (positive drop arm sign).
4. The empty can test reproduces pain and weakness; external rotation strength is 3/5 on the right; there is no joint effusion.
5. MRI of the right shoulder shows a full-thickness supraspinatus tendon tear with 2 cm of retraction and moderate fatty infiltration of the supraspinatus muscle.
6. Given the full-thickness tear with significant retraction and his functional demands as a carpenter, he undergoes arthroscopic rotator cuff repair and returns to full function at 6 months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most commonly torn tendon: supraspinatus (initiates abduction 0-15 degrees); also infraspinatus (external rotation), subscapularis (internal rotation), teres minor.
- Painful arc: pain with abduction from 60-120 degrees as supraspinatus passes under the acromion.
- Drop arm sign: inability to slowly lower the arm from 90 degrees abduction; indicates large or complete tear.
- MRI is most sensitive for diagnosis; CT arthrography is an alternative when MRI is contraindicated.
- Treatment: conservative (PT, injections) for partial tears or low-demand patients; surgical repair for full-thickness tears in active patients.

CASE_ID: 191
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Somatic Symptom Disorder

ALIASES:
somatization disorder
functional somatic syndrome
psychosomatic disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old woman presents for the 12th time in 2 years with multiple unexplained physical complaints including chest pain, abdominal pain, headaches, fatigue, and back pain despite extensive negative workup.
2. She has seen 8 different specialists and undergone numerous procedures including laparoscopy, cardiac catheterization, and multiple MRIs, all of which were normal.
3. She spends 2-3 hours daily researching her symptoms online and feels her doctors are missing something serious; the symptoms cause significant functional impairment.
4. PHQ-15 score is 20 (severe); she meets DSM-5 criteria for somatic symptom disorder with excessive thoughts, feelings, and behaviors related to her symptoms lasting more than 6 months.
5. All metabolic, inflammatory, and structural workup is again negative during this visit.
6. She is engaged in a therapeutic relationship with a single primary care physician; cognitive behavioral therapy is initiated; a low-dose SNRI is started; unnecessary further workup is avoided.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- DSM-5 criteria: one or more somatic symptoms causing distress PLUS excessive thoughts, feelings, or behaviors related to those symptoms — does NOT require absence of medical explanation.
- Differs from illness anxiety disorder (hypochondriasis): SSD has prominent physical symptoms; illness anxiety has minimal symptoms but excessive fear of disease.
- Conversion disorder: neurological symptoms (paralysis, blindness, seizures) without neurological disease; positive functional signs on exam.
- Factitious disorder: intentional production of symptoms for the sick role (not external gain); Munchausen syndrome by proxy involves inducing illness in others.
- Treatment: CBT (most evidence), regular scheduled visits with single provider, avoid unnecessary testing, low-dose antidepressants.

CASE_ID: 192
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Delirium

ALIASES:
acute confusional state
ICU delirium
acute delirium

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 78-year-old man with mild baseline dementia admitted for hip fracture repair is noted on post-op day 2 to be pulling at his IV lines, not recognizing his family, and speaking incoherently at 2 AM.
2. He was calm and oriented preoperatively; his family says he was at baseline mental status the morning of surgery.
3. On exam, he is agitated, combative, and disoriented to time, place, and person; he has visual hallucinations and fluctuating level of consciousness throughout the day.
4. Temperature is 38.2°C; oxygen saturation is 92% on room air; urinalysis shows pyuria and bacteriuria; bladder scan shows 420 mL of retained urine.
5. His pain is poorly controlled and he received diphenhydramine for sleep the prior evening.
6. Urinary catheter is placed relieving retention; antibiotics are started for UTI; opioids and diphenhydramine are discontinued; non-pharmacologic sleep hygiene, reorientation, early mobilization, and haloperidol PRN for agitation are implemented.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acute confusional state: fluctuating consciousness + inattention + disorganized thinking; develops over hours to days; always has an underlying cause.
- Hypoactive delirium is more common and often missed (quiet, withdrawn); hyperactive is more dramatic but less common.
- DELIRIUM mnemonic for causes: Drugs, Eyes and ears (sensory impairment), Low O2, Infection, Retention (urine or stool), Ictal, Underhydration and undernutrition, Metabolic disturbances.
- Risk factors: advanced age, dementia, hospitalization, polypharmacy, immobility, sleep deprivation, sensory impairment.
- Treatment: treat underlying cause first; non-pharmacologic interventions (reorientation, mobilization, day-night cycle preservation); antipsychotics sparingly for severe agitation only.

CASE_ID: 193
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Seborrheic Dermatitis

ALIASES:
seborrhea
dandruff
cradle cap

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man presents with a 2-year history of itchy flaky skin on his scalp, eyebrows, nasolabial folds, and ears that flares with stress and improves in summer.
2. He has tried multiple over-the-counter shampoos with only partial improvement; his scalp has thick greasy yellow scales.
3. On exam, erythematous patches with overlying greasy yellowish scales are present on the scalp, eyebrows, nasolabial folds, glabella, and external ear canals bilaterally.
4. KOH preparation of scalp scale shows hyphae and spores consistent with Malassezia furfur.
5. He has no joint symptoms, nail changes, or plaques on the elbows or knees; the greasy quality and sebaceous distribution distinguish this from psoriasis.
6. He is started on ketoconazole 2% shampoo twice weekly plus selenium sulfide for the scalp, and topical low-potency hydrocortisone for the face; symptoms improve significantly within 3 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Common chronic inflammatory dermatosis associated with Malassezia yeast overgrowth; affects sebaceous gland-rich areas.
- Distribution: scalp (dandruff), eyebrows, nasolabial folds, external ear canals, central chest, glabella.
- Worsens with stress, cold and dry weather; associated with Parkinson disease and HIV.
- Infantile form: cradle cap (thick greasy scalp scales in infants within the first 3 months of life).
- Treatment: antifungal shampoos (ketoconazole, selenium sulfide, zinc pyrithione); topical corticosteroids for inflammation; may require ongoing maintenance therapy.

CASE_ID: 194
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: dermatology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lichen Planus

ALIASES:
LP
oral lichen planus
lichenoid dermatosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old man presents with a 3-month history of intensely pruritic flat-topped violaceous bumps on his wrists and ankles; he also has white lacy streaks inside his cheeks for 6 months.
2. He started hydrochlorothiazide for hypertension 6 months ago.
3. On exam, multiple flat-topped, polygonal, violaceous papules and plaques on the flexor surfaces of the wrists and ankles are present; Wickham striae (white lacy lines) are visible on the surface of the papules.
4. The oral mucosa shows bilateral white lacy reticulations (Wickham striae) on the buccal mucosa without erosions.
5. Skin biopsy shows a band-like lymphocytic infiltrate at the dermoepidermal junction with saw-toothing of the rete ridges and civatte bodies (apoptotic keratinocytes).
6. Hydrochlorothiazide is switched to amlodipine; he is started on high-potency topical corticosteroids and oral antihistamines; lesions gradually resolve over 4 months.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- The 6 P's: Pruritic, Purple (violaceous), Polygonal, Planar (flat-topped), Papules, Plaques.
- Wickham striae: white lacy lines on surface of papules — pathognomonic for lichen planus.
- Oral lichen planus: white reticulations on buccal mucosa; erosive form has malignant potential.
- Drug-induced lichenoid reactions: thiazides, antimalarials, beta-blockers, ACE inhibitors, NSAIDs, gold.
- Koebner phenomenon: new lesions appear at sites of skin trauma.
- Treatment: topical corticosteroids; systemic steroids for severe widespread disease; phototherapy (PUVA).

CASE_ID: 195
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Juvenile Idiopathic Arthritis

ALIASES:
JIA
juvenile rheumatoid arthritis
oligoarticular JIA

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 5-year-old girl is brought in for a limp and swelling of her right knee noticed 6 weeks ago; she is most stiff in the morning and improves as the day progresses.
2. She has no fever; her parents report she sometimes refuses to walk in the morning but is running by the afternoon.
3. On exam, the right knee is swollen with a palpable effusion and warmth; range of motion is mildly limited; the left ankle also has a small effusion.
4. WBC, ESR, and CRP are mildly elevated; ANA is positive at 1:160; rheumatoid factor and anti-CCP are both negative.
5. Slit-lamp exam by ophthalmology reveals asymptomatic anterior uveitis in both eyes — completely unknown to the parents.
6. She is diagnosed with oligoarticular JIA and started on naproxen and methotrexate; ophthalmology follow-up every 3 months is arranged given the high uveitis risk in ANA-positive oligoarticular JIA.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common chronic rheumatic disease in children; onset before age 16, duration >6 weeks.
- Oligoarticular JIA: fewer than 5 joints affected; ANA positive; HIGHEST risk of chronic anterior uveitis (often asymptomatic — regular slit-lamp screening is essential).
- Systemic JIA (Still disease): quotidian (daily) spiking fever, salmon-colored evanescent rash, arthritis, hepatosplenomegaly, serositis; risk of macrophage activation syndrome.
- RF-positive polyarticular JIA: similar to adult RA; anti-CCP positive; worst long-term prognosis.
- Treatment: NSAIDs, methotrexate, biologics (TNF inhibitors, IL-6 inhibitors, IL-1 inhibitors for systemic JIA).

CASE_ID: 196
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Henoch-Schonlein Purpura

ALIASES:
HSP
IgA vasculitis
Henoch-Schönlein purpura

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 8-year-old boy presents with 4 days of a non-blanching rash on his legs and buttocks, bilateral ankle and knee pain, and crampy abdominal pain after recovering from an upper respiratory infection 2 weeks ago.
2. His parents note the rash does not blanch when pressed; he has had two episodes of blood in his urine today.
3. On exam, palpable purpura is symmetrically distributed over his buttocks, lower extremities, and ankles; bilateral ankle and knee swelling is present; the abdomen is diffusely tender without peritoneal signs.
4. Urinalysis shows hematuria with 2+ protein; CBC shows normal platelets (ruling out thrombocytopenic purpura); creatinine is normal; coagulation studies are normal.
5. Skin biopsy shows IgA and C3 deposits in vessel walls on immunofluorescence with leukocytoclastic vasculitis.
6. He is managed supportively with NSAIDs for joint pain; nephrology monitors his renal function weekly; he recovers fully over 8 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common systemic vasculitis in children; IgA-mediated small vessel vasculitis; often follows upper respiratory infection.
- Tetrad: palpable purpura (non-thrombocytopenic) + arthritis + abdominal pain + renal disease (hematuria and proteinuria).
- Palpable purpura: non-blanching, lower extremities and buttocks — pathognomonic distribution.
- Skin biopsy: IgA deposits in vessel walls (same IgA deposits in glomeruli as IgA nephropathy — Berger disease is the adult equivalent).
- Usually self-limited in children; monitor renal function closely; steroids for severe abdominal or renal involvement.

CASE_ID: 197
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Wilms Tumor

ALIASES:
nephroblastoma
pediatric kidney tumor
Wilms' tumor

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 3-year-old girl is brought in by her parents who noticed a large bulge in her left abdomen while giving her a bath; she has been eating and behaving normally.
2. She has no hematuria, fever, or weight loss; her parents note hemihypertrophy of her left leg since birth.
3. On exam, a large firm non-tender mass is palpable in the left flank that does not cross the midline; blood pressure is 148/92 mmHg.
4. Urinalysis shows microscopic hematuria; abdominal ultrasound shows a large intrarenal mass distorting the left kidney.
5. CT confirms a 9 cm well-encapsulated heterogeneous left renal mass; the right kidney appears normal; no vascular invasion is seen.
6. She undergoes left radical nephrectomy; pathology shows triphasic Wilms tumor (blastemal, stromal, and epithelial components) with favorable histology; she receives adjuvant actinomycin D and vincristine with excellent prognosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common renal malignancy in children; peak age 3-4 years; usually presents as an asymptomatic abdominal mass.
- Associated syndromes: WAGR (Wilms, Aniridia, GU anomalies, intellectual disability — WT1 deletion), Beckwith-Wiedemann (hemihypertrophy, macroglossia — WT2 locus), Denys-Drash.
- Do NOT palpate the abdomen repeatedly — risk of tumor capsule rupture and peritoneal seeding.
- Histology: blastemal, stromal, epithelial components; favorable histology confers >90% 5-year survival.
- Treatment: nephrectomy + chemotherapy (actinomycin D, vincristine) ± radiation for unfavorable histology or advanced stage.

CASE_ID: 198
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Testicular Torsion

ALIASES:
torsion of the testis
scrotal emergency
bell-clapper deformity

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 15-year-old boy is brought to the ED at 2 AM with sudden onset severe right scrotal pain that woke him from sleep; he has vomited twice.
2. He had a similar brief episode of right scrotal pain 3 weeks ago that resolved spontaneously within an hour (intermittent torsion).
3. On exam, the right testicle is markedly tender, swollen, and elevated with a transverse (horizontal) lie; the cremasteric reflex is absent on the right.
4. Elevation of the scrotum does not relieve the pain (negative Prehn sign); Doppler ultrasound shows absent blood flow to the right testicle.
5. He is taken directly to the operating room without delay for further workup; at 4 hours from onset the right testicle is found twisted 540 degrees but is still viable after detorsion.
6. Bilateral orchiopexy is performed to prevent future torsion on either side; he is discharged on post-op day 1.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Urological emergency; testicular viability: >90% if detorsion within 6 hours; <10% at 24 hours from onset.
- Bell-clapper deformity: high attachment of the tunica vaginalis allows the testis to swing freely and twist.
- Most common in adolescents; absent cremasteric reflex is the most sensitive sign.
- Doppler ultrasound confirms absent flow but do NOT delay surgery for imaging if clinical suspicion is high.
- Bilateral orchiopexy: both testes must be fixed because the contralateral testis has the same anatomical predisposition.

CASE_ID: 199
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Cholangiocarcinoma

ALIASES:
bile duct cancer
Klatskin tumor
hilar cholangiocarcinoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old man with a 20-year history of primary sclerosing cholangitis presents with 3 months of progressively worsening painless jaundice, weight loss, and right upper quadrant discomfort.
2. He has lost 22 pounds unintentionally and reports dark urine and clay-colored stools.
3. On exam, he is deeply jaundiced; the gallbladder is NOT palpable; the liver edge is firm and nodular.
4. CA 19-9 is markedly elevated at 1,840 U/mL; total bilirubin is 14.2 mg/dL; alkaline phosphatase is 680 U/L.
5. MRCP shows a stricture at the hepatic hilum at the confluence of the right and left hepatic ducts (Klatskin tumor) with upstream biliary dilation.
6. ERCP with brush cytology confirms adenocarcinoma; portal vein involvement is found on staging; biliary stenting is performed for palliation and he is started on gemcitabine plus cisplatin chemotherapy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Malignancy of bile duct epithelium; strongly associated with PSC, choledochal cysts, Caroli disease, liver flukes (Clonorchis, Opisthorchis).
- Klatskin tumor: hilar cholangiocarcinoma at the confluence of the right and left hepatic ducts — most common location.
- Painless progressive jaundice + weight loss + elevated CA 19-9 and alkaline phosphatase.
- Courvoisier law: palpable non-tender gallbladder + jaundice = malignant biliary obstruction (NOT gallstones, which cause a fibrotic, non-distensible gallbladder).
- Prognosis is poor; surgical resection offers the only potential cure but most present with unresectable disease.

CASE_ID: 200
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Pancreatic Cancer

ALIASES:
pancreatic adenocarcinoma
exocrine pancreatic cancer
pancreatic ductal adenocarcinoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 67-year-old man presents with a 3-month history of painless progressive jaundice, dark urine, clay-colored stools, and a 25-pound weight loss.
2. He also reports new-onset diabetes mellitus diagnosed 4 months ago and dull epigastric pain radiating to the back relieved by leaning forward.
3. On exam, he is deeply jaundiced; the gallbladder is palpable and non-tender in the right upper quadrant (Courvoisier sign).
4. CA 19-9 is markedly elevated at 4,200 U/mL; CT abdomen shows a 3.4 cm hypodense mass in the pancreatic head with dilation of both the common bile duct and pancreatic duct (double duct sign).
5. No vascular involvement is seen; CT chest and abdomen show no distant metastases.
6. He undergoes a Whipple procedure (pancreaticoduodenectomy); pathology confirms ductal adenocarcinoma with negative margins; adjuvant gemcitabine plus capecitabine chemotherapy is started.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common pancreatic malignancy; head of pancreas (70%) causes obstructive jaundice.
- Courvoisier sign: palpable non-tender gallbladder + jaundice = malignant biliary obstruction (not gallstones).
- Double duct sign on CT or MRCP: dilation of both the common bile duct and pancreatic duct at the head — highly suspicious for pancreatic head cancer.
- Risk factors: smoking (most important modifiable), chronic pancreatitis, diabetes, obesity, BRCA2 mutation, Lynch syndrome.
- New-onset diabetes in an older patient can be a paraneoplastic manifestation of pancreatic cancer; always consider.

CASE_ID: 201
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hypoparathyroidism

ALIASES:
post-surgical hypoparathyroidism
hypocalcemia from PTH deficiency
surgical hypoparathyroidism

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 48-year-old woman presents 2 days after total thyroidectomy with perioral numbness, tingling in her fingers and toes, and muscle cramps in both hands.
2. She reports an uncomfortable sensation of her hands cramping involuntarily when she drives with her hands on the steering wheel (carpopedal spasm).
3. On exam, tapping the facial nerve anterior to the ear causes ipsilateral facial muscle contraction (positive Chvostek sign); inflating a blood pressure cuff above systolic for 3 minutes causes carpal spasm (positive Trousseau sign).
4. Serum calcium is 6.8 mg/dL (low); phosphate is 5.8 mg/dL (elevated); PTH is undetectable; magnesium is normal.
5. ECG shows prolonged QTc interval of 510 ms from hypocalcemia.
6. She is given IV calcium gluconate acutely with prompt symptom relief; she is maintained on oral calcium carbonate and calcitriol (active vitamin D); calcium normalizes over 2 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause: inadvertent parathyroid removal or devascularization during thyroid or parathyroid surgery.
- Hypocalcemia + hyperphosphatemia + low or absent PTH = hypoparathyroidism.
- Symptoms of hypocalcemia: perioral numbness, paresthesias, carpopedal spasm, tetany, laryngospasm, seizures, prolonged QTc.
- Chvostek sign: facial twitch with tapping CN VII; Trousseau sign: carpal spasm with BP cuff inflation (more specific for hypocalcemia).
- Treatment: IV calcium gluconate acutely for symptomatic hypocalcemia; oral calcium plus calcitriol for chronic management.

CASE_ID: 202
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Myotonic Dystrophy

ALIASES:
myotonic dystrophy type 1
DM1
Steinert disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old man presents with progressive hand weakness and difficulty releasing his grip after shaking hands; he also reports excessive daytime sleepiness and difficulty swallowing.
2. His father had similar hand problems and died of a cardiac arrhythmia at age 48; his infant son has congenital hypotonia.
3. On exam, he has a characteristic facies with frontal balding, temporal wasting, ptosis, and a hatchet-shaped face; he cannot promptly release a handshake (grip myotonia).
4. Percussion of the thenar eminence causes a sustained local muscle contraction that slowly relaxes (percussion myotonia); EMG shows classic dive-bomber myotonic discharges.
5. Slit-lamp exam shows posterior subcapsular cataracts bilaterally; ECG shows a first-degree AV block.
6. Genetic testing reveals a CTG trinucleotide repeat expansion in the DMPK gene with 680 repeats (normal <37); he is counseled on anticipation and referred for multisystem management including cardiology.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common adult muscular dystrophy; autosomal dominant CTG repeat expansion in DMPK gene on chromosome 19.
- Myotonia: inability to relax muscles after voluntary contraction; worse in cold; grip myotonia and percussion myotonia.
- Multisystem disease: distal-predominant weakness (opposite of most myopathies), cataracts, cardiac conduction defects (most common cause of death), diabetes, hypogonadism, frontal balding, hypersomnia.
- Anticipation: longer CTG repeats in successive generations leads to earlier onset and more severe disease.
- EMG: waxing and waning dive-bomber myotonic discharges; treatment includes mexiletine for myotonia; cardiac pacemaker or ICD as needed.

CASE_ID: 203
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Lambert-Eaton Myasthenic Syndrome

ALIASES:
LEMS
Lambert-Eaton syndrome
paraneoplastic neuromuscular disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old male smoker presents with a 4-month history of proximal leg weakness making it difficult to rise from a chair, dry mouth, and constipation.
2. He notes his strength actually improves briefly with repeated use before fatiguing — the opposite pattern to myasthenia gravis.
3. On exam, proximal leg weakness is prominent; deep tendon reflexes are absent throughout but briefly return after sustained muscle contraction (post-tetanic potentiation).
4. He has autonomic features including dry mouth, constipation, and erectile dysfunction; there is no ptosis or diplopia.
5. EMG shows an incremental response with high-frequency stimulation (>100% increase in CMAP amplitude); anti-VGCC (voltage-gated calcium channel) antibodies are positive.
6. CT chest reveals a central lung mass; biopsy confirms small cell lung cancer; treating the underlying SCLC leads to partial improvement in LEMS; 3,4-diaminopyridine is added for symptomatic benefit.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Autoimmune attack on presynaptic voltage-gated calcium channels at the neuromuscular junction; most commonly paraneoplastic from SCLC.
- Distinguished from myasthenia gravis: proximal leg weakness (not ocular), reflexes absent but return post-exercise (facilitation), prominent autonomic features, incremental EMG response (vs. decremental in MG).
- Autonomic features: dry mouth, constipation, hypohidrosis, erectile dysfunction — important distinguishing features.
- Anti-VGCC antibodies are highly specific; always search for SCLC with chest CT when LEMS is diagnosed.
- Treatment: treat underlying malignancy; 3,4-diaminopyridine; pyridostigmine; IVIG or plasmapheresis for refractory cases.

CASE_ID: 204
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Renovascular Hypertension

ALIASES:
renal artery stenosis
fibromuscular dysplasia hypertension
secondary hypertension from renal artery

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with severe hypertension (178/108 mmHg) discovered incidentally; she has no family history of hypertension and takes no medications.
2. She has no headaches, sweating episodes, or visual changes; she does not smoke; she is otherwise healthy.
3. On exam, a bruit is heard over the right flank; blood pressure is equal in both arms.
4. She is started on lisinopril; within 48 hours her creatinine rises from 0.7 to 1.8 mg/dL and potassium rises to 5.6 mEq/L suggesting renal artery stenosis.
5. CT angiography shows a beaded appearance of the right renal artery with multiple areas of stenosis and dilation in the mid and distal segments — classic for fibromuscular dysplasia.
6. She undergoes percutaneous transluminal angioplasty of the right renal artery; blood pressure normalizes over 3 months and ACE inhibitor is discontinued.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Two main causes: atherosclerosis (older patients, proximal renal artery) and fibromuscular dysplasia (young women, mid and distal artery, beaded appearance on imaging).
- Renal artery stenosis reduces renal perfusion → RAAS activation → renovascular hypertension.
- ACE inhibitor-induced renal failure: in bilateral RAS or solitary kidney RAS, blocking the efferent arteriole tone causes a precipitous drop in GFR.
- Flank bruit + refractory hypertension in a young patient + ACE inhibitor-induced AKI = renovascular hypertension workup.
- FMD treatment: percutaneous angioplasty without stenting; atherosclerotic RAS: medical management preferred over revascularization.

==================================================

CASE_ID: 205
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology_oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Burkitt Lymphoma

ALIASES:
Burkitt's lymphoma
endemic Burkitt lymphoma
c-MYC lymphoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. An 8-year-old boy in sub-Saharan Africa presents with a rapidly growing jaw mass that has developed over 3 weeks; his mother first noticed it as a small swelling that has dramatically enlarged.
2. He has had recurrent malaria infections over the past 2 years; he appears malnourished.
3. On exam, a massive hard rapidly growing mass deforms the right side of his mandible; cervical lymphadenopathy is present; a palpable mass is present in the right lower quadrant of the abdomen.
4. LDH is markedly elevated at 1,840 U/L; uric acid is elevated at 9.8 mg/dL; WBC is normal.
5. Bone marrow biopsy shows a starry sky pattern with large cells interspersed with tingible body macrophages; immunophenotyping confirms B-cell lymphoma with Ki-67 >99%.
6. FISH reveals t(8;14) MYC-IGH translocation; he is started on intensive chemotherapy with aggressive tumor lysis syndrome prophylaxis using allopurinol and IV fluids; complete remission is achieved.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most rapidly dividing human tumor (Ki-67 nearly 100%); associated with EBV (especially endemic form) and c-MYC translocation.
- Three forms: endemic (Africa, jaw mass, EBV-associated), sporadic (US, abdominal mass, often ileocecal), immunodeficiency-associated (HIV).
- Starry sky pattern on biopsy: sheets of lymphoma cells with scattered macrophages ingesting apoptotic debris.
- t(8;14) translocation juxtaposes c-MYC with the immunoglobulin heavy chain locus driving uncontrolled proliferation.
- Tumor lysis syndrome: major risk with treatment; prophylaxis with allopurinol or rasburicase plus aggressive IV hydration is mandatory.

CASE_ID: 206
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Toxoplasmosis

ALIASES:
cerebral toxoplasmosis
Toxoplasma gondii
CNS toxoplasmosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 35-year-old man with HIV (CD4 count 58) presents with 2 weeks of progressive headache, confusion, and new left-sided weakness; he is not on any prophylaxis or ART.
2. He has a cat at home and handles cat litter regularly; a Toxoplasma IgG antibody was positive 6 months ago.
3. On exam, he is febrile at 38.3°C with left hemiparesis and left facial droop; he is confused and disoriented.
4. MRI of the brain with gadolinium shows multiple ring-enhancing lesions in the basal ganglia and thalamus bilaterally with surrounding edema and a characteristic eccentric target sign.
5. Serum Toxoplasma IgM is negative but IgG is strongly positive; CSF analysis shows mild lymphocytic pleocytosis and elevated protein.
6. Empiric pyrimethamine plus sulfadiazine plus leucovorin is started; at 2-week follow-up MRI shows significant reduction in lesion size confirming the diagnosis without biopsy.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause of focal brain lesion in AIDS; occurs at CD4 <100; reactivation of latent infection.
- Ring-enhancing lesions in basal ganglia with eccentric target sign; multiple lesions favor toxoplasmosis over CNS lymphoma (usually solitary).
- Cats are the definitive hosts; oocysts shed in cat feces; also transmitted via undercooked meat.
- Empiric treatment response at 2 weeks is diagnostic; if no improvement, brain biopsy to rule out CNS lymphoma.
- Treatment: pyrimethamine + sulfadiazine + leucovorin (folinic acid prevents bone marrow suppression); prophylaxis with TMP-SMX when CD4 <100.

CASE_ID: 207
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Influenza

ALIASES:
flu
influenza A
influenza B

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old man with COPD and diabetes presents in December with abrupt onset fever of 39.6°C, severe myalgias, headache, dry cough, and profound malaise that began 18 hours ago.
2. He received a flu vaccine 4 months ago; he lives in a nursing home where several residents have had similar illnesses this week.
3. On exam, he appears toxic and fatigued; respiratory rate is 22/min; oxygen saturation is 93% on room air; lungs have diffuse expiratory wheezes.
4. Rapid influenza antigen test from a nasopharyngeal swab is positive for influenza A; chest X-ray shows bilateral peribronchial thickening without focal consolidation.
5. He is started on oseltamivir within 48 hours of symptom onset given his high-risk status; his COPD exacerbation is treated concurrently with bronchodilators and steroids.
6. He improves over 5 days; blood cultures and procalcitonin remain negative ruling out bacterial superinfection.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Orthomyxovirus; seasonal epidemics; abrupt onset distinguishes from other respiratory viruses.
- Classic: abrupt onset fever + severe myalgias + headache + dry cough + malaise; constitutional symptoms predominate over respiratory.
- Complications: primary influenza pneumonia, secondary bacterial pneumonia (Staph aureus most dangerous), myocarditis, encephalitis, Reye syndrome with aspirin in children.
- Treatment: oseltamivir (Tamiflu) within 48 hours is most effective; always treat high-risk patients regardless of timing.
- Antigenic drift: minor annual changes from point mutations (seasonal flu); antigenic shift: major reassortment of gene segments (pandemic potential).

CASE_ID: 208
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Dupuytren Contracture

ALIASES:
Dupuytren's disease
palmar fibromatosis
palmar contracture

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 62-year-old man of Northern European descent presents with progressive inability to fully extend his right ring and little fingers over 3 years; he noticed a firm cord developing in his palm.
2. He drinks alcohol heavily, has had diabetes for 10 years, and his father had a similar condition treated surgically.
3. On exam, a firm longitudinal cord is palpable in the palm extending to the base of the right ring and little fingers; fixed flexion contractures of 30 degrees at the MCP and 20 degrees at the PIP joint are present.
4. There is no tenderness, erythema, or warmth; Hueston tabletop test is positive (patient cannot place palm flat on the table).
5. Histopathology shows proliferation of fibroblasts and myofibroblasts producing type III collagen in the palmar fascia.
6. He undergoes collagenase clostridium histolyticum (Xiaflex) injection with enzymatic dissolution of the cord; after manual rupture he achieves near-complete extension of both fingers.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Fibroproliferative disorder of the palmar fascia; ring and little fingers most commonly affected.
- Risk factors: Northern European descent, alcohol use, diabetes, epilepsy (phenytoin), trauma, male sex, family history.
- Hueston tabletop test: positive when the palm cannot be placed flat on a table — indicates significant contracture.
- Progression: palmar nodule → longitudinal cord → fixed flexion contracture (cannot extend finger).
- Treatment: collagenase injection (Xiaflex), needle fasciotomy, or surgical fasciectomy for contracture >30 degrees at MCP joint.

CASE_ID: 209
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Osgood-Schlatter Disease

ALIASES:
tibial tubercle apophysitis
knee pain adolescent
Osgood-Schlatter syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 13-year-old soccer player presents with a 2-month history of pain and swelling just below his right knee that is worse with running, jumping, and stair climbing and improves with rest.
2. He has recently significantly increased his training intensity for an upcoming season.
3. On exam, there is point tenderness and prominent swelling directly over the right tibial tubercle; pain is reproducible with resisted knee extension; range of motion is full and the knee joint itself is not tender or swollen.
4. X-ray of the right knee shows fragmentation and irregularity of the tibial tubercle apophysis with soft tissue swelling anterior to the tibial tuberosity.
5. He is managed conservatively with activity modification, ice, NSAIDs, and quadriceps and hamstring stretching; he is cleared to continue sports as tolerated.
6. Symptoms resolve completely within 12-18 months as the apophysis fuses; he is counseled that a bony prominence at the tibial tubercle may persist permanently.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Traction apophysitis of the tibial tubercle from repetitive patellar tendon stress during periods of rapid skeletal growth.
- Most common in active adolescents (boys 10-15, girls 8-13) during growth spurts; bilateral in 30% of cases.
- Point tenderness and bony prominence over the tibial tubercle are the key clinical findings.
- X-ray: fragmentation of the tibial tubercle apophysis; do not confuse with acute avulsion fracture.
- Self-limiting: resolves when the apophysis fuses at skeletal maturity; conservative management only.

CASE_ID: 210
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Substance Use Disorder

ALIASES:
alcohol use disorder
cocaine use disorder
stimulant use disorder

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man is brought by his wife who reports he has been using cocaine almost daily for 2 years; he recently lost his job due to absenteeism and their marriage is under severe strain.
2. He has tried to stop multiple times on his own but is unable to; he spends most of his day obtaining or recovering from cocaine.
3. On exam, he has nasal septal perforation, elevated blood pressure, and mild weight loss; he is agitated and has paranoid ideation.
4. He endorses spending over $2,000 per month on cocaine, lying to his family, missing his children's events, and using cocaine in dangerous situations.
5. Urine toxicology is positive for cocaine metabolites; ECG shows sinus tachycardia without ischemic changes; troponin is negative.
6. He is referred for intensive outpatient treatment with motivational interviewing and contingency management; he agrees to attend one session.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- DSM-5: 2 or more of 11 criteria within a 12-month period — impaired control, social impairment, risky use, pharmacological (tolerance and withdrawal).
- Cocaine: blocks reuptake of dopamine, norepinephrine, and serotonin; sympathomimetic toxidrome; nasal septal perforation from vasoconstriction with intranasal use.
- Cocaine withdrawal: crash (dysphoria, fatigue, hypersomnia, increased appetite) — NOT life-threatening unlike alcohol or benzodiazepine withdrawal.
- Alcohol and benzodiazepine withdrawal: life-threatening (seizures, delirium tremens); always medically manage with benzodiazepines.
- Treatment: behavioral therapies (CBT, contingency management) are first-line for stimulant use disorder; no FDA-approved pharmacotherapy for cocaine use disorder.

CASE_ID: 211
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: psychiatry

DIAGNOSIS:
Bulimia Nervosa

ALIASES:
bulimia
binge-purge disorder
eating disorder bulimia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 20-year-old college woman presents requesting help for a pattern of binge eating followed by self-induced vomiting that she has engaged in secretly for 2 years.
2. She describes consuming large amounts of food rapidly with a sense of loss of control, followed by intense guilt and shame that drives her to purge.
3. On exam, her weight is in the normal range (BMI 22); bilateral parotid gland enlargement is noted; calluses are present on the dorsum of her right hand (Russell sign from self-induced vomiting).
4. Labs show hypokalemia 2.8 mEq/L, metabolic alkalosis, low chloride, and mildly elevated salivary amylase; ECG shows prolonged QTc from hypokalemia.
5. Dental exam reveals significant enamel erosion on the lingual surfaces of the upper teeth from recurrent acid exposure.
6. She is started on high-dose fluoxetine (the only FDA-approved medication for bulimia nervosa) and referred for CBT; at 3 months binge-purge frequency has decreased by 70%.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Recurrent binge eating + compensatory behaviors (purging, laxatives, fasting, excessive exercise) with normal or near-normal weight; unlike anorexia where weight is markedly low.
- Electrolyte abnormalities from purging: hypokalemia, hypochloremia, metabolic alkalosis, elevated amylase.
- Physical signs: Russell sign (knuckle calluses from self-induced vomiting), parotid hypertrophy, dental enamel erosion.
- Treatment: CBT is most effective; fluoxetine 60 mg/day is FDA-approved; avoid bupropion (lowers seizure threshold in eating disorders).
- Distinguish from anorexia: weight is normal in bulimia; anorexia has markedly low body weight as a defining criterion.

CASE_ID: 212
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Cervical Cancer

ALIASES:
cervical carcinoma
HPV-related cervical cancer
squamous cell carcinoma cervix

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 42-year-old woman presents with a 4-month history of postcoital bleeding, intermenstrual spotting, and a malodorous vaginal discharge; she has not had a Pap smear in 10 years.
2. She had first sexual intercourse at age 15 and has had multiple sexual partners; she has never received HPV vaccination.
3. On exam, speculum examination reveals a friable exophytic mass on the anterior lip of the cervix measuring 3 cm that bleeds on contact; bimanual exam shows parametrial thickening.
4. Biopsy of the cervical mass confirms moderately differentiated squamous cell carcinoma; HPV genotyping shows HPV type 16.
5. MRI pelvis shows the cervical mass invading the parametria bilaterally without pelvic sidewall involvement; CT chest and abdomen show no distant metastases (FIGO stage IIB).
6. She is treated with concurrent cisplatin-based chemotherapy and external beam radiation therapy with intracavitary brachytherapy boost.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause: high-risk HPV (types 16 and 18 account for approximately 70% of cases).
- Risk factors: multiple sexual partners, early onset of sexual activity, smoking, immunosuppression (HIV), lack of screening.
- Screening: Pap smear with or without HPV co-testing; CIN (cervical intraepithelial neoplasia) is the precursor lesion.
- Most common histologic type: squamous cell carcinoma (80%); adenocarcinoma accounts for 15-20%.
- Prevention: HPV vaccine (Gardasil 9) is highly effective against HPV types 16, 18, 6, 11, and others; ideally given before sexual debut.

CASE_ID: 213
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Ovarian Cancer

ALIASES:
epithelial ovarian cancer
high-grade serous ovarian carcinoma
ovarian carcinoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old postmenopausal woman presents with 3 months of increasing abdominal girth, early satiety, bloating, and a 15-pound weight loss.
2. She has a family history of ovarian cancer in her mother and breast cancer in her sister; she is nulliparous and never used oral contraceptives.
3. On exam, the abdomen is distended with shifting dullness indicating ascites; a firm irregular pelvic mass is palpable on bimanual exam.
4. CA-125 is markedly elevated at 2,840 U/mL; CT abdomen and pelvis shows bilateral complex ovarian masses with peritoneal implants and omental caking.
5. Paracentesis yields serous fluid with malignant cells on cytology; BRCA1 mutation is found on germline genetic testing.
6. She undergoes optimal cytoreductive surgery followed by carboplatin-paclitaxel chemotherapy with olaparib (PARP inhibitor) maintenance therapy given her BRCA1 mutation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most lethal gynecologic malignancy; often diagnosed at advanced stage (III or IV) due to vague nonspecific symptoms.
- Symptoms: bloating, abdominal distension, early satiety, pelvic pain, urinary symptoms — subtle and nonspecific.
- Risk factors: nulliparity, BRCA1 and BRCA2 mutations (highest risk), Lynch syndrome, family history, early menarche and late menopause.
- Protective factors: oral contraceptive use (reduces risk by 50% with prolonged use), multiparity, breastfeeding, tubal ligation, salpingectomy.
- Most common type: high-grade serous carcinoma; PARP inhibitors (olaparib) are effective maintenance therapy in BRCA-mutated patients.

CASE_ID: 214
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Spinal Cord Compression

ALIASES:
metastatic spinal cord compression
epidural spinal cord compression
MSCC

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 64-year-old man with known metastatic prostate cancer presents with 3 weeks of progressive mid-back pain and 3 days of bilateral leg weakness and difficulty walking.
2. He reports urinary hesitancy and constipation beginning 2 days ago; he has been dropping objects and his legs feel heavy and stiff.
3. On exam, he has spastic paraparesis (3/5 strength bilaterally), hyperreflexia, bilateral Babinski signs, and a sensory level at T8 (loss of all sensation below the umbilicus).
4. Rectal tone is reduced; bladder scan shows 540 mL of retained urine; MRI emergency order is placed.
5. Emergency MRI of the entire spine shows a T7 vertebral metastasis with epidural tumor compressing the spinal cord; cord signal change indicates myelopathy.
6. He is started immediately on high-dose dexamethasone; emergency radiation therapy is delivered within 24 hours; surgical decompression is discussed given his ambulatory status at presentation.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Oncological emergency; most common cancers causing cord compression: prostate, breast, lung, renal cell, multiple myeloma.
- Back pain is the earliest and most common symptom; neurological deficits (weakness, sensory level, bladder and bowel dysfunction) follow and can be irreversible.
- MRI of the entire spine is mandatory because multiple levels may be involved simultaneously.
- Treatment: high-dose dexamethasone immediately upon suspicion; urgent radiation therapy; surgical decompression for selected patients.
- Prognosis for ambulation: if ambulatory at time of treatment, 80% remain ambulatory; if paraplegic at treatment, fewer than 10% regain ambulation.
