CASE_ID: 224
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Uterine Fibroids

ALIASES:
leiomyoma
uterine leiomyoma
myoma

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old African American woman presents with a 2-year history of increasingly heavy prolonged menstrual periods lasting 10-12 days with large clots, pelvic pressure, and urinary frequency.
2. She has tried ibuprofen without sufficient relief; hemoglobin is 9.2 g/dL from iron deficiency secondary to chronic menorrhagia.
3. On exam, the uterus is enlarged, irregular, and nontender measuring approximately 14 weeks size; no adnexal masses are appreciated.
4. Pelvic ultrasound shows multiple intramural and submucosal fibroids, the largest measuring 6.8 cm, with distortion of the endometrial cavity.
5. MRI of the pelvis confirms multiple leiomyomas with the dominant submucosal fibroid significantly distorting the uterine cavity; endometrial biopsy shows benign endometrium.
6. She undergoes uterine artery embolization with significant reduction in fibroid size and menstrual blood loss at 3-month follow-up.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common benign gynecologic tumor; most common in African American women (3-9x higher risk).
- Estrogen and progesterone dependent; regress after menopause; worsen during pregnancy.
- Submucosal fibroids: most symptomatic (heavy bleeding, infertility); intramural: bulk symptoms; subserosal: least symptomatic.
- Symptoms: heavy menstrual bleeding (most common), pelvic pressure, urinary frequency and urgency, constipation, infertility.
- Treatment options: GnRH agonists (medical, temporary shrinkage), uterine artery embolization, myomectomy (preserves fertility), hysterectomy (definitive); progesterone-releasing IUD reduces bleeding.

CASE_ID: 225
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Erectile Dysfunction

ALIASES:
ED
impotence
male sexual dysfunction

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 58-year-old man with hypertension, type 2 diabetes, and a 30 pack-year smoking history presents requesting help for inability to achieve and maintain erections sufficient for intercourse that has been worsening over 2 years.
2. He reports morning erections have decreased significantly; he is able to achieve partial erections occasionally with direct stimulation but cannot maintain them.
3. On exam, blood pressure is 148/92 mmHg; he has decreased penile sensation; pedal pulses are diminished bilaterally suggesting peripheral vascular disease.
4. Testosterone is low-normal at 280 ng/dL; HbA1c is 9.2% indicating poor glycemic control; LDL is 168 mg/dL; PHQ-9 score is 12 (moderate depression).
5. Penile Doppler ultrasound after prostaglandin E1 injection shows reduced peak systolic velocity consistent with arteriogenic erectile dysfunction.
6. He is started on sildenafil; glycemic and lipid control are optimized; smoking cessation is strongly encouraged; depression is treated; he reports significant improvement at 3-month follow-up.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common cause in men over 40: vascular disease (atherosclerosis); ED is often an early marker of systemic cardiovascular disease.
- Risk factors: diabetes (most important modifiable), hypertension, smoking, dyslipidemia, obesity, depression, medications (beta-blockers, SSRIs, thiazides, 5-alpha reductase inhibitors).
- Psychogenic ED: sudden onset, preserved nocturnal and morning erections, situational.
- Organic ED: gradual onset, absent morning erections, affects all situations.
- Treatment: lifestyle modification, optimize comorbidities; PDE-5 inhibitors (sildenafil, tadalafil) are first-line; vacuum erection devices, intracavernosal injections, penile prosthesis for refractory cases.

CASE_ID: 226
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Absence Seizure

ALIASES:
petit mal seizure
childhood absence epilepsy
typical absence seizure

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 7-year-old girl is referred by her teacher who reports she has episodes of staring blankly for 5-15 seconds occurring dozens of times per day during which she is unresponsive and then immediately resumes normal activity.
2. She is unaware of these episodes; her parents notice she sometimes stops mid-sentence and continues as if nothing happened.
3. On exam, hyperventilation for 3 minutes triggers a 10-second episode of blank staring with subtle eyelid fluttering followed by immediate return to full consciousness; she has no memory of the episode.
4. Neurological exam is completely normal; MRI of the brain is normal.
5. EEG shows characteristic 3 Hz generalized spike-and-wave discharges on a normal background that are provoked by hyperventilation and photic stimulation.
6. She is started on ethosuximide (drug of choice for pure absence seizures); at 3 months seizure frequency has decreased by 95%; she is expected to outgrow her epilepsy by adolescence.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Generalized non-convulsive seizures with abrupt onset and offset; no postictal confusion (distinguishes from complex partial seizures).
- Brief staring spells (5-30 seconds) with subtle automatic behaviors (lip smacking, eyelid fluttering); can occur dozens to hundreds of times per day.
- Triggered by hyperventilation in the office (very useful diagnostic maneuver); EEG shows 3 Hz generalized spike-and-wave discharges.
- Childhood absence epilepsy: onset 4-12 years; often outgrown by adolescence; normal intelligence and normal MRI.
- Treatment: ethosuximide (drug of choice for pure absence); valproate if concurrent generalized tonic-clonic seizures; avoid carbamazepine and oxcarbazepine (worsen absence seizures).

CASE_ID: 227
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Horner Syndrome

ALIASES:
oculosympathetic paresis
ptosis miosis anhidrosis
Horner's syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 55-year-old man presents with a droopy right eyelid and a constricted right pupil first noticed this morning; he also reports right-sided neck and face pain after a chiropractic neck manipulation yesterday.
2. He has mild right-sided headache and dizziness; the symptoms began acutely after the manipulation.
3. On exam, right-sided partial ptosis, miosis (2 mm right vs 4 mm left), and anhidrosis of the right face are present; the anisocoria is worse in dim light.
4. The ptosis is partial (Muller muscle involvement, not the full levator); there is no diplopia; the remainder of the cranial nerve exam is normal.
5. MRI and MR angiography of the neck show a right internal carotid artery dissection with an intimal flap at the C2 level.
6. He is anticoagulated with heparin bridged to warfarin; brain MRI shows no stroke; his Horner syndrome partially resolves over 4 weeks.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Classic triad: partial ptosis (Muller muscle) + miosis + ipsilateral facial anhidrosis (if preganglionic or central lesion).
- Anisocoria is worse in dim light (the affected pupil fails to dilate); cocaine drops confirm Horner syndrome.
- Central (first-order neuron): stroke, syringomyelia, Wallenberg syndrome, demyelination; Preganglionic (second-order): Pancoast tumor, cervical rib, neck dissection; Postganglionic (third-order): carotid artery dissection, cavernous sinus lesion, cluster headache.
- Carotid artery dissection is a critical cause to identify — associated with neck trauma and stroke risk.
- Hydroxyamphetamine drops localize the lesion: if postganglionic the pupil fails to dilate (depleted norepinephrine stores).

==================================================

CASE_ID: 228
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Migraine

ALIASES:
migraine with aura
migraine headache
common migraine

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 28-year-old woman presents with a 6-year history of recurrent severe headaches occurring 3-4 times per month, each lasting 12-24 hours and significantly impairing her ability to work.
2. Each headache is unilateral, throbbing, and severe (8/10); it is worsened by physical activity and accompanied by nausea, vomiting, photophobia, and phonophobia.
3. She sees a shimmering arc of zigzag lights in her visual field that gradually expands over 20 minutes and then disappears 30 minutes before each headache (visual aura).
4. Between episodes, neurological exam is completely normal; she has a family history of migraines in her mother and sister.
5. MRI of the brain is normal; she has been using ibuprofen and acetaminophen more than 15 days per month and is now experiencing medication overuse headache.
6. She is started on topiramate for prevention; sumatriptan is prescribed for acute attacks; analgesics are strictly limited to fewer than 10 days per month; at 3 months frequency decreases from 4 to 1 episode per month.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common type of disabling primary headache; unilateral, pulsating, moderate to severe intensity, worsened by routine physical activity, with nausea and photo- or phonophobia.
- Aura: cortical spreading depression causing reversible neurological symptoms (visual most common); precedes headache by 20-60 minutes.
- Medication overuse headache (MOH): analgesics used more than 10-15 days per month cause rebound headaches; withdrawal is necessary.
- Acute treatment: triptans (5-HT1B/1D agonists) first-line for moderate to severe; NSAIDs, antiemetics for mild to moderate.
- Prevention (4 or more migraines per month): topiramate, valproate, propranolol, amitriptyline, CGRP antagonists (erenumab, fremanezumab).

==================================================

CASE_ID: 229
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: nephrology

DIAGNOSIS:
Contrast-Induced Nephropathy

ALIASES:
contrast-induced AKI
radiocontrast nephropathy
contrast nephropathy

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old man with CKD (baseline creatinine 2.2 mg/dL), diabetes, and peripheral vascular disease undergoes CT angiography with 180 mL of iodinated contrast without pre-hydration.
2. He is asymptomatic but 48 hours post-procedure his creatinine has risen from 2.2 to 3.8 mg/dL; urine output has decreased.
3. Urinalysis shows granular muddy brown casts and tubular epithelial cells consistent with acute tubular injury; urine sodium is 38 mEq/L and FENa is 2.4%.
4. Renal ultrasound shows no obstruction; he has no fever and urine culture is negative.
5. He is managed with IV normal saline hydration; creatinine peaks at 4.2 mg/dL at day 3 and returns to 2.4 mg/dL by day 10 without requiring dialysis.
6. He is counseled on minimizing future contrast use and ensuring adequate pre-hydration; nephrology is consulted regarding his progressive CKD.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- AKI occurring within 48-72 hours of intravascular contrast administration; peak creatinine at 3-5 days; usually reversible within 2 weeks.
- Risk factors: pre-existing CKD (most important risk factor), diabetes, volume depletion, heart failure, large contrast volume, concurrent nephrotoxic medications.
- Pathogenesis: direct tubular toxicity combined with renal vasoconstriction leads to ischemic acute tubular necrosis.
- Prevention: IV saline hydration before and after procedure; minimize contrast volume; use low-osmolar or iso-osmolar contrast agents; hold nephrotoxic drugs.
- N-acetylcysteine: controversial but commonly used empirically given low risk and potential benefit.

CASE_ID: 230
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology_oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Hemophilia B

ALIASES:
factor IX deficiency
Christmas disease
X-linked factor IX deficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 5-year-old boy is evaluated for recurrent joint bleeding in his knees and elbows after minor injuries; he had prolonged bleeding requiring hospitalization after circumcision at age 8 days.
2. His maternal grandfather had similar bleeding problems throughout his life; his mother is a known carrier.
3. Labs show markedly prolonged aPTT with normal PT, normal platelet count, and normal bleeding time; mixing study corrects the aPTT (indicating factor deficiency rather than inhibitor).
4. Factor VIII activity is normal at 98%; factor IX activity is 2% of normal, confirming the diagnosis of Hemophilia B.
5. Genetic testing of the F9 gene on the X chromosome confirms a pathogenic mutation; his mother's factor IX level is 52% consistent with carrier status.
6. He is enrolled in a comprehensive hemophilia treatment center; recombinant factor IX concentrate is prescribed for prophylaxis and on-demand treatment; gene therapy is discussed as a future option.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- X-linked recessive; factor IX deficiency; clinically indistinguishable from Hemophilia A (factor VIII deficiency) without specific factor assay.
- Hemophilia A: factor VIII deficiency; Hemophilia B (Christmas disease): factor IX deficiency.
- Prolonged aPTT + normal PT + normal platelet count = intrinsic pathway defect; factor assay is needed to differentiate.
- Hemarthroses (most common manifestation) and deep muscle hematomas; intracranial hemorrhage is the leading cause of death.
- Treatment: recombinant factor IX concentrate; gene therapy (etranacogene dezaparvovec) is now FDA-approved for adults with Hemophilia B.

CASE_ID: 231
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Candidiasis

ALIASES:
oral thrush
esophageal candidiasis
oropharyngeal candidiasis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 38-year-old man with HIV (CD4 count 120) presents with 2 weeks of painful white plaques in his mouth and throat making it difficult to eat; he also has odynophagia when swallowing.
2. He ran out of his ART medications 3 months ago; he has lost 8 pounds from poor oral intake.
3. On exam, white curd-like plaques are present on the tongue, buccal mucosa, and posterior pharynx; the plaques are easily scraped off leaving an erythematous base that bleeds.
4. KOH preparation of scraped material shows pseudohyphae and budding yeast forms.
5. Given his odynophagia, esophageal involvement is suspected; upper endoscopy shows white plaques throughout the esophagus with a cheesy exudative appearance.
6. He is treated with systemic fluconazole 150 mg daily for 14-21 days; his ART is restarted; oral candidiasis resolves within 1 week and he is able to eat normally.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common fungal infection in immunocompromised patients; Candida albicans most common species.
- Oral thrush: white curd-like plaques on erythematous base; easily scraped off (distinguishes from oral hairy leukoplakia which cannot be scraped off).
- Esophageal candidiasis: odynophagia + thrush in an immunocompromised patient = treat empirically with fluconazole without requiring endoscopy.
- Vulvovaginal candidiasis: thick white cottage cheese discharge, pruritus, erythema; treat with topical or oral azoles; recurrent disease may indicate diabetes or HIV.
- Treatment: topical nystatin for mild oropharyngeal; fluconazole for moderate-severe, esophageal, or systemic; echinocandins for fluconazole-resistant or invasive candidiasis.

CASE_ID: 232
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Whooping Cough

ALIASES:
pertussis
Bordetella pertussis
pertussis syndrome

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-week-old unvaccinated infant is brought to the ED with paroxysmal coughing episodes ending with a high-pitched inspiratory whoop and post-tussive vomiting; between paroxysms she appears well.
2. She has had a runny nose and low-grade fever for 1 week; her father had a prolonged persistent cough 3 weeks ago.
3. On exam, she is afebrile; during a coughing paroxysm her face turns red and she briefly becomes cyanotic; the characteristic whoop is heard at the end of each paroxysm.
4. WBC is 42,000 with 80% lymphocytes (marked lymphocytosis); chest X-ray shows a shaggy heart border.
5. Nasopharyngeal PCR for Bordetella pertussis is positive; the infant is placed on droplet precautions.
6. She is admitted given her young age and started on azithromycin; household contacts are given azithromycin prophylaxis; her father is confirmed as the source from waning vaccine immunity.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Three stages: catarrhal (1-2 weeks, URI symptoms, most contagious), paroxysmal (2-6 weeks, classic whooping cough), convalescent (weeks to months, gradual improvement).
- Marked lymphocytosis (WBC >20,000 with lymphocyte predominance) is characteristic and helps distinguish from other respiratory infections.
- Most severe in unvaccinated infants <6 months; can cause apnea, pneumonia, pulmonary hypertension, encephalopathy, death.
- Infants may not have the characteristic whoop; apnea after coughing paroxysms is the more common presentation in very young infants.
- Treatment: azithromycin (macrolides); post-exposure prophylaxis for close contacts; DTaP vaccine for children, Tdap booster for adolescents and adults.

CASE_ID: 233
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Avascular Necrosis

ALIASES:
osteonecrosis
AVN of the femoral head
aseptic necrosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old man with SLE on long-term prednisone presents with a 4-month history of progressive right hip pain that is worse with weight bearing and walking.
2. He has no history of trauma or prior hip problems; he also drinks alcohol heavily on weekends.
3. On exam, internal rotation of the right hip reproduces pain; range of motion is limited and painful at the extremes; the hip is not acutely tender to palpation.
4. Plain X-ray of the right hip is initially normal; MRI is ordered given clinical suspicion.
5. MRI shows a crescent-shaped subchondral hypointense band in the right femoral head with surrounding bone marrow edema — the crescent sign indicating subchondral fracture and impending collapse.
6. He is referred to orthopedic surgery; core decompression is performed; his prednisone dose is reduced to the minimum effective dose and alcohol cessation is strongly recommended.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Death of bone tissue from disrupted blood supply; most common site is the femoral head.
- Risk factors: corticosteroids (most common cause), alcohol, sickle cell disease, trauma (femoral neck fracture), SLE, Gaucher disease, radiation, caisson disease (diving), pregnancy.
- MRI is the most sensitive early test (before plain X-ray changes); crescent sign indicates subchondral collapse.
- Stages: early (normal X-ray, abnormal MRI) → bone sclerosis → subchondral crescent → collapse → joint space loss.
- Treatment: core decompression for early disease; total hip replacement for advanced or collapsed femoral head.

CASE_ID: 234
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Paget Disease of Bone

ALIASES:
osteitis deformans
Paget's disease of bone
skeletal Paget disease

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old man is referred after an elevated alkaline phosphatase of 480 U/L on routine labs; he reports mild left leg pain and has noticed his head size has increased over the years.
2. He wears a larger hat than he did 20 years ago and has noted his left leg appears bowed; he has mild bilateral hearing loss.
3. On exam, the left tibia has an anterior-lateral bow; the skull appears enlarged; bilateral sensorineural hearing loss is present on Rinne and Weber testing.
4. Alkaline phosphatase is markedly elevated at 480 U/L; serum calcium, phosphate, and PTH are all normal; urine hydroxyproline is elevated.
5. X-rays show thickened coarsened trabecular pattern in the left tibia with cortical thickening and blade-of-grass sign at the lytic front; skull X-ray shows cotton wool spots and calvarial thickening.
6. He is treated with IV zoledronic acid (bisphosphonate); ALP normalizes over 3 months and symptoms improve significantly.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Excessive and disorganized bone remodeling (increased osteoclast activity followed by osteoblast activity) producing enlarged but structurally weak bone.
- Most commonly affected bones: skull, pelvis, spine, femur, tibia.
- Complications: bone pain, pathological fractures, nerve compression (sensorineural hearing loss from CN VIII compression is most common), high-output heart failure, osteosarcoma (rare but life-threatening transformation).
- Labs: markedly elevated ALP (reflecting increased bone turnover); serum calcium is normal unless patient is immobilized.
- Treatment: bisphosphonates (IV zoledronic acid or oral alendronate) suppress osteoclast activity; very effective at reducing ALP and symptoms.

CASE_ID: 235
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Organophosphate Poisoning

ALIASES:
cholinergic toxidrome
acetylcholinesterase inhibitor toxicity
pesticide poisoning

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 45-year-old farm worker is brought to the ED after spraying pesticides without adequate protective equipment; he has increasing salivation, tearing, and difficulty breathing over 2 hours.
2. He has had several episodes of diarrhea and urinated involuntarily in the ambulance.
3. On exam, pupils are pinpoint bilaterally; he is bradycardic at 42 bpm; diffuse wheezing and copious secretions are present; he is diaphoretic with muscle fasciculations throughout.
4. Oxygen saturation is 82% on room air; he is confused and increasingly lethargic; a garlic-like odor is noted from his clothing.
5. He is immediately decontaminated (clothing removed, skin washed); IV atropine is titrated until secretions dry.
6. Pralidoxime (2-PAM) is given to reactivate acetylcholinesterase; he is intubated for respiratory failure; after 48 hours of treatment he is extubated and recovers.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Organophosphates inhibit acetylcholinesterase causing acetylcholine accumulation at muscarinic and nicotinic receptors.
- SLUDGE (muscarinic effects): Salivation, Lacrimation, Urination, Defecation, GI cramping, Emesis; plus bradycardia, bronchospasm, miosis.
- Nicotinic effects: muscle fasciculations, weakness, paralysis; respiratory muscle failure is the primary cause of death.
- Treatment: decontamination first (protect healthcare workers); atropine (blocks muscarinic effects — titrate to drying secretions, NOT pupil size); pralidoxime (reactivates AChE — must be given early before irreversible aging occurs).
- Sarin and VX are nerve agents that act by the same mechanism; used as chemical weapons.

CASE_ID: 236
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Tricyclic Antidepressant Overdose

ALIASES:
TCA overdose
tricyclic overdose
amitriptyline toxicity

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old man with depression is found unresponsive with an empty bottle of amitriptyline; he had a tonic-clonic seizure in the ambulance on the way to the ED.
2. On exam, he is unresponsive (GCS 6); pupils are dilated at 6 mm bilaterally; heart rate is 138 bpm; blood pressure is 84/52 mmHg; skin is warm and dry.
3. ECG shows sinus tachycardia with QRS widening to 160 ms, rightward axis deviation with R wave in aVR >3 mm, and QTc prolongation to 540 ms.
4. Arterial blood gas shows pH 7.22 with metabolic acidosis.
5. He is intubated for airway protection; IV sodium bicarbonate bolus and infusion are given achieving a serum pH of 7.50-7.55 with immediate QRS narrowing to 110 ms.
6. Dopamine is started for refractory hypotension; he is monitored in the ICU for 24 hours without further arrhythmias and is extubated on day 2.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- TCA toxicity: sodium channel blockade (QRS widening, arrhythmias, hypotension) + anticholinergic effects (tachycardia, mydriasis, dry skin, urinary retention, ileus) + alpha-1 blockade (hypotension) + CNS depression.
- ECG: QRS >100 ms is concerning; QRS >160 ms indicates high arrhythmia risk; R wave in aVR >3 mm is a key finding.
- Sodium bicarbonate is the most important treatment: alkalinization and increased sodium concentration reverse sodium channel blockade and narrow the QRS.
- Avoid physostigmine (can cause cardiac arrest in TCA overdose); avoid class Ia antiarrhythmics (worsen sodium channel blockade).
- Seizures: treated with benzodiazepines; sodium bicarbonate also reduces seizure risk by alkalinizing the CNS.

CASE_ID: 237
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Testicular Cancer

ALIASES:
seminoma
testicular germ cell tumor
nonseminomatous germ cell tumor

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 26-year-old man presents with a 2-month history of painless left testicular enlargement and heaviness that he initially dismissed; he has a history of cryptorchidism repaired surgically at age 2.
2. He denies trauma or fever; the swelling has been gradually increasing.
3. On exam, the left testicle is enlarged and firm with a hard non-tender nodule on the posterior surface; transillumination does not show light transmission through the mass.
4. Serum tumor markers: beta-hCG markedly elevated at 4,200 mIU/mL and AFP elevated at 1,840 ng/mL; LDH is elevated; scrotal ultrasound confirms a hypoechoic intratesticular mass.
5. CT chest, abdomen, and pelvis shows retroperitoneal lymphadenopathy measuring up to 3.2 cm; no visceral metastases are seen.
6. Radical orchiectomy via inguinal approach is performed; pathology shows mixed germ cell tumor (embryonal carcinoma plus teratoma); he is staged as IIB and treated with BEP chemotherapy (bleomycin, etoposide, cisplatin).

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common solid tumor in men aged 20-35; cryptorchidism is the strongest risk factor.
- Classic: painless testicular mass; transillumination negative (unlike hydrocele which transilluminates).
- Tumor markers: AFP is not produced by pure seminoma (elevated AFP means nonseminomatous component); beta-hCG produced by both seminoma and NSGCT; LDH reflects tumor burden.
- Pure seminoma: most common, radiosensitive, excellent prognosis; treated with surveillance or radiation for stage I.
- Orchiectomy via inguinal approach is mandatory; scrotal approach alters lymphatic drainage and is contraindicated.

CASE_ID: 238
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Abdominal Aortic Aneurysm

ALIASES:
AAA
aortic aneurysm
infrarenal aortic aneurysm

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 72-year-old male smoker with hypertension and hyperlipidemia is found to have an infrarenal aortic aneurysm measuring 5.8 cm on routine ultrasound screening; he is completely asymptomatic.
2. His father died at age 68 from a ruptured aortic aneurysm; he has smoked one pack per day for 40 years.
3. On exam, a pulsatile expansile midline abdominal mass is palpable just above the umbilicus; bruits are heard over the mass.
4. CT angiography confirms an infrarenal AAA measuring 5.9 cm in maximum diameter with mural thrombus and no evidence of rupture or leakage.
5. Cardiology clearance is obtained; he undergoes elective endovascular aortic aneurysm repair (EVAR) with deployment of an endograft.
6. Postoperative CT confirms good graft position with no endoleak; he is discharged on post-operative day 2 and enrolled in annual CT surveillance.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Defined as aortic diameter >3 cm; most commonly in the infrarenal location.
- Risk factors: male sex, age >65, smoking (most important modifiable), hypertension, atherosclerosis, family history.
- Classic triad of rupture: severe back or flank pain + hypotension + pulsatile abdominal mass — 50% of ruptured AAAs die before reaching hospital.
- Screening: one-time abdominal ultrasound for men aged 65-75 who have ever smoked (USPSTF recommendation).
- Elective repair: EVAR or open surgery when >5.5 cm in men or >5 cm in women; ruptured AAA is an immediate surgical emergency.

CASE_ID: 239
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: gastroenterology

DIAGNOSIS:
Budd-Chiari Syndrome

ALIASES:
hepatic vein thrombosis
hepatic venous outflow obstruction

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman presents with a 3-week history of right upper quadrant pain, progressive abdominal distension, and jaundice; she takes oral contraceptive pills and was recently diagnosed with polycythemia vera.
2. She has no prior liver disease or alcohol use; she appears acutely ill with rapidly developing massive ascites.
3. On exam, the liver is markedly enlarged and tender; massive ascites is present; splenomegaly is noted; lower extremity edema is present.
4. Labs show elevated bilirubin (6.2 mg/dL), elevated transaminases, elevated alkaline phosphatase, INR 1.9, and albumin 2.6 g/dL.
5. Doppler ultrasound of the liver shows absent or reversed flow in the hepatic veins; the caudate lobe is hypertrophied (receives independent venous drainage directly into the IVC).
6. CT angiography confirms thrombosis of all three hepatic veins; she is anticoagulated with LMWH and undergoes TIPS for portal decompression with significant improvement.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Hepatic vein thrombosis causing post-sinusoidal portal hypertension; caudate lobe is spared because it drains directly into the IVC.
- Causes: hypercoagulable states (polycythemia vera is the most common, JAK2 V617F mutation), factor V Leiden, antiphospholipid syndrome, PNH, OCP use, malignancy.
- Classic triad: abdominal pain + tender hepatomegaly + ascites; caudate lobe hypertrophy on imaging is characteristic.
- CT or MRI with contrast: non-visualization of hepatic veins; Doppler ultrasound shows absent or reversed hepatic venous flow.
- Treatment: anticoagulation; TIPS for portal hypertension and refractory ascites; liver transplant for acute liver failure.

CASE_ID: 240
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: endocrinology

DIAGNOSIS:
Vitamin D Deficiency

ALIASES:
hypovitaminosis D
rickets
osteomalacia
vitamin D insufficiency

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 4-year-old boy from Somalia presents with bowing of both legs that his family noticed when he started walking; he has also had several fractures from minor falls.
2. He is exclusively breastfed and rarely goes outdoors; no vitamin D supplementation has ever been given.
3. On exam, he has genu varum (bowlegs), enlargement of the wrists and ankles, rachitic rosary at the costochondral junctions, frontal bossing, and delayed tooth eruption.
4. Labs show low 25-OH vitamin D at 8 ng/mL; calcium is low-normal at 8.2 mg/dL; phosphate is low at 2.1 mg/dL; ALP is markedly elevated at 680 U/L; PTH is elevated at 184 pg/mL (secondary hyperparathyroidism).
5. X-rays show cupping and fraying of the metaphyses of the distal radius and ulna with widened growth plates — classic rachitic changes.
6. He is started on high-dose vitamin D3 supplementation plus calcium; at 6-month follow-up bowing has improved and ALP has normalized.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Vitamin D deficiency leads to impaired calcium and phosphate absorption and inadequate bone mineralization.
- Rickets: children with open growth plates; osteomalacia: adults with closed growth plates (bone pain and proximal muscle weakness).
- Risk factors: dark skin, exclusive breastfeeding without supplementation, limited sun exposure, malabsorption (celiac, Crohn, gastric bypass).
- Labs: low 25-OH vitamin D + low calcium + low phosphate + elevated ALP + elevated PTH (secondary hyperparathyroidism).
- X-ray findings in rickets: cupping, fraying, and widening of growth plates; treatment is vitamin D3 plus calcium supplementation.

CASE_ID: 241
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: hematology_oncology

DIAGNOSIS:
Paroxysmal Nocturnal Hemoglobinuria

ALIASES:
PNH
complement-mediated hemolysis
PIGA mutation hemolysis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 34-year-old woman presents with a 6-month history of intermittent dark brown morning urine, fatigue, and episodic abdominal pain; she has had recurrent venous thrombosis.
2. She also reports a severe headache last month attributed to her menstrual cycle; her complete blood count shows persistent anemia.
3. On exam, she is pale and mildly jaundiced; splenomegaly is present; no lymphadenopathy.
4. CBC shows hemoglobin 8.2 g/dL with elevated reticulocytes; LDH is markedly elevated at 2,400 U/L; haptoglobin is undetectable; direct Coombs test is negative.
5. Urine hemosiderin is positive indicating chronic intravascular hemolysis; flow cytometry shows a large clone of RBCs and granulocytes lacking CD55 and CD59.
6. She is started on eculizumab (C5 complement inhibitor) with dramatic reduction in hemolysis and thrombotic risk; she receives meningococcal vaccine before starting eculizumab.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Acquired clonal disorder from somatic PIGA mutation → lack of GPI-anchored complement regulatory proteins (CD55 and CD59) → unregulated complement activation → intravascular hemolysis.
- Classic: dark morning urine (hemoglobinuria from overnight complement activation), Coombs-negative hemolytic anemia, thrombosis in unusual sites (hepatic veins causing Budd-Chiari, mesenteric veins, cerebral veins).
- Diagnosis: flow cytometry showing absence of CD55 and CD59 on blood cells (Ham test is historical).
- Associated with aplastic anemia and can transform to AML.
- Treatment: eculizumab (anti-C5 monoclonal antibody) prevents complement-mediated hemolysis and dramatically reduces thrombotic risk; meningococcal vaccination mandatory before treatment.

CASE_ID: 242
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: infectious_disease

DIAGNOSIS:
Listeria Monocytogenes

ALIASES:
listeriosis
Listeria infection
neonatal listeriosis

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old woman at 28 weeks pregnant presents with a 3-day history of fever, chills, myalgias, and headache; she recalls eating deli meats and soft cheese at a party last week.
2. She reports decreased fetal movement over the past 24 hours; she is very concerned.
3. On exam, temperature is 39.1°C; she appears mildly ill; fetal heart rate monitoring shows intermittent decelerations.
4. Blood cultures are drawn; CBC shows leukocytosis with 18% monocytes; lumbar puncture shows 280 WBCs (predominantly monocytes), elevated protein, and low glucose.
5. Gram stain of CSF shows short gram-positive rods; blood cultures subsequently grow Listeria monocytogenes.
6. She is started on high-dose IV ampicillin plus gentamicin; she delivers a premature but surviving infant who is treated empirically for neonatal listeriosis.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Facultative intracellular gram-positive rod that grows at refrigerator temperatures (4°C) and in high salt.
- High-risk groups: pregnant women, neonates, elderly, immunocompromised patients, and those with iron overload states.
- Contaminated foods: deli meats, soft cheeses (brie, feta), unpasteurized dairy products, raw sprouts, cold smoked fish.
- Meningitis caused by Listeria shows monocytic CSF pleocytosis (unlike typical bacterial meningitis which shows neutrophilic pleocytosis).
- Treatment: ampicillin (or TMP-SMX if penicillin-allergic); intrinsically resistant to all cephalosporins; add gentamicin for synergistic bactericidal effect in severe cases.

CASE_ID: 243
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: musculoskeletal

DIAGNOSIS:
Slipped Capital Femoral Epiphysis

ALIASES:
SCFE
hip slip adolescent
capital femoral epiphysis slip

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 13-year-old obese boy presents with a 2-month history of left hip and thigh pain that worsened acutely yesterday when he fell during PE class; he is now unable to bear weight.
2. The pain radiates to his inner thigh and knee; he initially thought it was a knee problem.
3. On exam, he is obese (BMI 32); the left leg is held in external rotation at rest; internal rotation is markedly limited and painful; when the hip is flexed it obligatorily externally rotates (Drehmann sign).
4. Plain X-rays show widening of the left physeal plate with posterior and medial displacement of the femoral epiphysis relative to the femoral neck; Klein line shows the femoral head below the line.
5. Slip angle is 42 degrees on the lateral view indicating a moderate SCFE.
6. He is made non-weight bearing immediately and taken to the OR for in situ pinning; the right hip is also pinned prophylactically given the 20-40% risk of contralateral SCFE.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Displacement of the femoral epiphysis through the growth plate; most common hip disorder in adolescents.
- Risk factors: obesity (most important), male sex, African American race, hypothyroidism and other endocrine disorders (evaluate if bilateral or young age at presentation).
- Symptoms: hip, groin, thigh, or knee pain with limp; referred knee pain is classic and can lead to diagnostic delay.
- Obligatory external rotation with hip flexion (Drehmann sign) is pathognomonic.
- Treatment: urgent in situ pinning (non-weight bearing must begin immediately); avascular necrosis of the femoral head and chondrolysis are the major complications.

CASE_ID: 244
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pediatrics

DIAGNOSIS:
Legg-Calve-Perthes Disease

ALIASES:
Perthes disease
LCPD
avascular necrosis femoral head childhood

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-year-old boy presents with a 3-month history of painless limp and mild right hip pain that occasionally radiates to the right knee; he has been limping progressively more.
2. He is small for his age below the 5th percentile for height; he is otherwise healthy with no fever or trauma.
3. On exam, he is afebrile; internal rotation and abduction of the right hip are limited; Trendelenburg test is positive on the right; leg lengths are equal.
4. X-ray of the pelvis shows increased density and mild flattening of the right femoral head; the left hip is normal.
5. MRI of the hips confirms avascular necrosis of the right femoral head with a subchondral fracture crescent and surrounding bone marrow edema.
6. He is managed with activity restriction, NSAIDs, physical therapy, and close radiographic monitoring; his young age confers a favorable prognosis for femoral head remodeling.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Idiopathic avascular necrosis of the femoral head in children; peak age 4-8 years; male predominance (4:1); bilateral in 10-20%.
- Painless limp or mild hip and knee pain in a child; limitation of internal rotation and abduction.
- X-ray: increased density, flattening, and fragmentation of the femoral head; Catterall and Herring classifications guide prognosis.
- Younger age at onset (≤6 years) confers better prognosis due to greater remodeling potential.
- Treatment: containment (keeping femoral head within the acetabulum) with bracing or osteotomy for severe cases; conservative for most.

CASE_ID: 245
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: pharmacology

DIAGNOSIS:
Drug-Induced Lupus

ALIASES:
DILE
drug-induced lupus erythematosus
hydralazine-induced lupus

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 54-year-old man with hypertension treated with hydralazine for 18 months presents with a 2-month history of bilateral joint pain, morning stiffness, pleuritic chest pain, and fatigue.
2. He has no prior history of autoimmune disease; the joint symptoms are symmetric involving his hands and wrists.
3. On exam, bilateral hand joint swelling and tenderness without deformity are present; a pleural friction rub is heard at the right base.
4. ANA is positive at 1:320 (homogeneous pattern); anti-histone antibodies are strongly positive; anti-dsDNA is negative; complement C3 and C4 are normal.
5. CBC shows mild leukopenia; urinalysis is completely normal with no renal involvement.
6. Hydralazine is discontinued; his symptoms resolve completely over 6 weeks and ANA titers gradually decrease; he is switched to amlodipine for blood pressure management.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Drug-induced lupus: arthritis, serositis, and constitutional symptoms; renal and CNS involvement are rare (unlike idiopathic SLE).
- Most common causative drugs: hydralazine, procainamide, isoniazid, minocycline, anti-TNF agents (infliximab, etanercept).
- Anti-histone antibodies: most specific finding (positive in >95% of DILE); anti-dsDNA is usually NEGATIVE.
- Complement levels are normal in DILE (vs. active SLE where complement is consumed by immune complexes).
- Treatment: discontinue offending drug; NSAIDs for symptoms; corticosteroids for severe cases; complete resolution typically occurs within weeks to months.

CASE_ID: 246
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Acute Limb Ischemia

ALIASES:
arterial embolism limb
acute arterial occlusion
six P's of ischemia

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 68-year-old woman with atrial fibrillation on warfarin (INR was subtherapeutic at 1.4 last week) presents with sudden onset severe right leg pain, coldness, and pallor that began 3 hours ago.
2. The pain is excruciating and unlike anything she has felt before; the leg has become progressively numb and she can no longer move her toes.
3. On exam, the right leg is cold, pale, and mottled to the mid-thigh; no femoral, popliteal, or pedal pulses are palpable on the right; Doppler signals are absent in the right pedal vessels.
4. The right leg is painful to passive dorsiflexion of the toes (ischemic muscle); sensation is absent in the right foot; right dorsiflexion strength is absent.
5. She is anticoagulated with IV heparin immediately and taken emergently for catheter-directed thrombolysis followed by surgical embolectomy.
6. The leg is revascularized at 5 hours from onset; she requires fasciotomy for reperfusion compartment syndrome and recovers with minimal residual deficit.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Vascular emergency; the 6 P's: Pain, Pallor, Pulselessness, Paresthesias, Paralysis, Poikilothermia (cold).
- Most common cause: cardiac embolism (atrial fibrillation, mural thrombus after MI) or in-situ arterial thrombosis (atherosclerosis).
- Paralysis and paresthesias indicate advanced ischemia approaching irreversibility; time is critical for limb salvage.
- Immediate anticoagulation with heparin; revascularization via catheter-directed thrombolysis (if <14 days) or surgical embolectomy.
- Reperfusion injury after revascularization: compartment syndrome, hyperkalemia, myoglobinuria → fasciotomy and close electrolyte monitoring.

CASE_ID: 247
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Premature Ovarian Insufficiency

ALIASES:
POI
premature ovarian failure
early menopause

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 29-year-old woman presents with 8 months of irregular periods, hot flashes, night sweats, and vaginal dryness; her last period was 4 months ago and she is not pregnant.
2. Her sister had similar symptoms at age 32; she has no history of chemotherapy, radiation, or autoimmune disease.
3. On exam, she has mild vaginal atrophy; the remainder of her exam is normal.
4. FSH is markedly elevated at 68 mIU/mL on two measurements 4 weeks apart; LH is elevated; estradiol is markedly low at 18 pg/mL; prolactin and TSH are normal.
5. Pelvic ultrasound shows bilaterally small ovaries with a markedly reduced antral follicle count; karyotype is normal 46,XX.
6. Anti-ovarian antibodies are positive suggesting autoimmune etiology; she is counseled regarding infertility and started on hormone replacement therapy; egg donation is discussed for future fertility.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Loss of normal ovarian function before age 40; defined by FSH >25 mIU/mL on two occasions at least 4 weeks apart.
- Causes: idiopathic (most common), autoimmune, Turner syndrome (45,X — most common chromosomal cause), fragile X premutation, chemotherapy, radiation.
- Symptoms: menopausal symptoms (hot flashes, vaginal dryness) plus menstrual irregularity or amenorrhea in a young woman.
- Long-term consequences: osteoporosis, cardiovascular disease, premature mortality, infertility, psychological impact.
- Treatment: hormone replacement therapy until the natural age of menopause to reduce osteoporosis and cardiovascular risk; fertility options include egg donation and embryo donation.

CASE_ID: 248
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: surgery

DIAGNOSIS:
Perianal Abscess

ALIASES:
anorectal abscess
perirectal abscess
anal abscess

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 32-year-old man with Crohn disease presents with 4 days of severe perianal pain, throbbing swelling, and purulent drainage; he is febrile at 38.7°C.
2. He has been increasingly uncomfortable sitting and the drainage began this morning.
3. On exam, a fluctuant exquisitely tender erythematous swelling is present at the 5 o'clock position adjacent to the anus; crepitus is absent; no induration extends to the ischiorectal fossa.
4. WBC is 16,400; CT pelvis shows a 3.4 cm perianal abscess with no evidence of supralevator extension.
5. He undergoes incision and drainage in the ED under local anesthesia; 25 mL of purulent material is expressed; the wound is packed open.
6. Culture grows Bacteroides fragilis and E. coli; he is discharged on oral antibiotics with sitz bath instructions; a fistula-in-ano is identified at 6-week follow-up and surgically treated.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Most common anorectal emergency; cryptoglandular infection (infected anal glands at the dentate line) is the most common etiology.
- Perianal (most superficial and most common) → ischiorectal → intersphincteric → supralevator (deepest and most dangerous).
- Risk factors: Crohn disease, diabetes, HIV, immunosuppression, leukemia.
- Treatment: incision and drainage is mandatory and curative; antibiotics alone without drainage are inadequate.
- Fistula-in-ano develops in up to 50% after drainage; Crohn disease dramatically increases fistula risk and complexity.

CASE_ID: 249
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: reproductive

DIAGNOSIS:
Hyperemesis Gravidarum

ALIASES:
severe nausea and vomiting of pregnancy
HG

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 24-year-old woman at 9 weeks of her first pregnancy presents with severe intractable vomiting occurring 15-20 times per day for 3 weeks and inability to tolerate any oral intake.
2. She has tried ginger, vitamin B6, and promethazine suppositories without relief; she has lost 6 pounds since her last prenatal visit 3 weeks ago.
3. On exam, she is tachycardic at 112 bpm with dry mucous membranes, poor skin turgor, and orthostatic hypotension; she appears debilitated and distressed.
4. Labs show hypokalemia 2.8 mEq/L, metabolic alkalosis, elevated BUN to creatinine ratio from dehydration, and ketonuria; beta-hCG is very high at 248,000 mIU/mL.
5. TSH is suppressed (hCG-mediated transient gestational hyperthyroidism) but free T4 is only mildly elevated; thyroid antibodies are negative.
6. She is admitted for IV fluid resuscitation, IV potassium replacement, IV thiamine (before dextrose), ondansetron, and metoclopramide; after 48 hours she tolerates oral intake and is discharged.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Severe form of nausea and vomiting in pregnancy; defined by >5% pre-pregnancy weight loss plus ketonuria plus electrolyte disturbances.
- Occurs in first trimester; risk factors: multiple gestation, molar pregnancy, prior HG, female fetus.
- Wernicke encephalopathy risk: always give IV thiamine BEFORE dextrose in patients with prolonged vomiting.
- Transient gestational hyperthyroidism: TSH suppressed from hCG cross-reactivity with TSH receptor; usually self-limited, thyroid-specific treatment is NOT needed.
- Treatment: IV fluids (normal saline), electrolyte replacement, thiamine, antiemetics (ondansetron, metoclopramide, promethazine); TPN for severe refractory cases.

CASE_ID: 250
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: neurology

DIAGNOSIS:
Spinal Muscular Atrophy

ALIASES:
SMA
Werdnig-Hoffmann disease
SMN1 deletion

VIGNETTE_LINES:
1. A 6-month-old boy is brought in for evaluation because he cannot hold his head up, roll over, or sit with support; he was meeting milestones until age 4 months when his parents noticed progressive weakness.
2. He has a weak cry and difficulty breathing; he lies in a characteristic frog-leg position and can only move his fingers and toes.
3. On exam, he is alert with normal eye movements and facial expression; there is profound flaccid hypotonia and generalized weakness; fasciculations are visible in his tongue.
4. Deep tendon reflexes are absent throughout; paradoxical breathing is present (chest retracts during inspiration as the diaphragm contracts but intercostal muscles are too weak to expand the chest).
5. EMG shows denervation with fibrillation potentials; genetic testing reveals homozygous deletion of exon 7 of the SMN1 gene on chromosome 5q.
6. He is started on nusinersen (intrathecal antisense oligonucleotide) immediately; motor milestones previously thought impossible are achieved; gene therapy (onasemnogene abeparvovec) is also discussed.

TEACHING_POINTS:
- Autosomal recessive; SMN1 gene deletion on chromosome 5q → loss of survival motor neuron protein → anterior horn cell degeneration.
- SMA type 1 (Werdnig-Hoffmann): onset before 6 months; never sits independently; death from respiratory failure without treatment.
- Pure lower motor neuron disease: flaccid weakness, hypotonia, areflexia, tongue fasciculations; normal sensation distinguishes from other conditions.
- Paradoxical (bell-shaped chest) breathing from intercostal weakness with relatively preserved diaphragm function.
- Treatment: nusinersen (intrathecal), risdiplam (oral), onasemnogene abeparvovec (gene therapy — most effective when given early, ideally before symptoms); newborn screening is now universal in most US states.

CASE_ID: 346
DIFFICULTY: step1
SYSTEM: cardiology
DIAGNOSIS:
Atrial Flutter
ALIASES:
flutter
VIGNETTE_LINES:
- A 68-year-old man presents with palpitations and mild dyspnea.
- ECG shows a regular sawtooth pattern of atrial activity.
- Ventricular rate is approximately 150 bpm.
- He has a history of hypertension.
- No chest pain is reported.
- Rate control is initiated.
TEACHING_POINTS:
- Sawtooth flutter waves are classic.
- Usually due to atrial reentry circuit.
- Often treated similarly to atrial fibrillation.
